Builders Unscripted: Ep. 2 - Ashe Magalhaes, Founder of Hearth AI

OpenAI(1.9M) ·33:39 ·5.7K views · 2026-04-10
Ashe Magalhaes (Founder, Hearth AI) Romain Huet (Head of Developer Experience, OpenAI)
Ashe Magalhaes argues that AI is shifting the builder’s role from technical architect to "creative conductor," where agentic workflows enable individuals to build at unprecedented speeds while prioritizing human connection and presence.
TL;DR Ashe Magalhaes argues that AI is shifting the builder’s role from technical architect to "creative conductor," where agentic workflows enable individuals to build at unprecedented speeds while prioritizing human connection and presence. The Transition to Agents: Ashe...

TL;DR

Ashe Magalhaes argues that AI is shifting the builder’s role from technical architect to "creative conductor," where agentic workflows enable individuals to build at unprecedented speeds while prioritizing human connection and presence.

From Frontier Tech to Relational Intelligence

  • The Transition to Agents: Ashe founded Hearth AI in 2022 as the first "agentic CRM," attempting to build structured workflows with GPT-3.5 before tools like JSON schemas or structured outputs were available.
  • Engineering Rigor: Her background includes building and racing a solar-powered car at 60 mph through the Australian outback and working on satellites at NASA. These experiences instilled a high level of technical rigor that she now applies to non-deterministic AI systems.
  • Relational Intelligence Thesis: Inspired by the book *When Breath Becomes Air*, Ashe shifted her focus from "frontier tech" to connection. She believes AI should act as a "second brain" that extends cognitive limits to help humans feel more present and connected to one another.

The Agentic Personal Operating System

  • Slack as a Command Center: Ashe operates a private Slack workspace where she is the only human member.
      • Up-Managing: Agents are organized into channels (News, Birthdays, Goals, Money) and are tasked with "up-managing" her to reduce her screen time and help her stay focused on real-world interactions.
      • Observability: She uses a layer of instrumentation to monitor her agentic workflows, catching errors (e.g., infinite loading on a drafts page) and validating user behavior in real-time.
  • Rapid Prototyping: Her website, ashe.ai, features a "secrets page" where she prototypes ideas using "code-x 5-4."
      • One-Shot Extraction: Once a prototype is validated, she uses AI to extract specific chunks of code to create standalone products or open-source repositories (e.g., "Aesthetic Video").

Building in Public and the "Artist Archetype"

  • Creativity as the North Star: Ashe views the modern builder as closer to an artist than a traditional engineer. She advocates for "building while connected," using public feedback loops on X (Twitter) to drive product direction.
  • The "Burst" Workflow: Unlike the 11-hour marathons of traditional engineering, Ashe works in creative bursts. She uses AI to handle the "marathon" aspects of coding—like authentication layers and Stripe integrations—allowing her to focus on "taste" and "vibe."
  • Authenticity Premium: In an era of AI-generated noise, Ashe believes there is a premium on vulnerability and human "hand-off." Users respond to products that feel like they have a "piece of the builder" in them.

The Future of Hardware and Distribution

  • Social Etiquette in Wearables: Ashe is exploring the frontier of software-hardware integration. She envisions wearables that act as ambient intelligence but emphasizes the need for new social norms (e.g., physical cues to indicate when a conversation is being recorded into a "second brain").
  • Automated Distribution: To manage the noise of multi-platform marketing, she uses OpenCall to distribute her content.
      • Platform Specificity: Agents translate her core ideas into the specific "language" of platforms like Reddit or LinkedIn to ensure they don't feel like spam.

Notable Quotes

"I view the builder archetype as quite close to the artist archetype. I think I'm happiest viewing myself as a conduit for creativity, and I think engineering for me has always felt like a tool for creating and incarnating." — Ashe Magalhaes
"The arc of human progress bends towards love and connection and innovation, and AI—I believe—should push the needle on that." — Ashe Magalhaes
"This is the moment to lean into creativity as the North Star... you are incentivized to bring your full self to the table as a builder." — Ashe Magalhaes
Hey Ashe, so great to see you. Thank you for doing this. Thank you for having me. It's awesome to be here. I actually remember the very first time we met was in this OpenAI office and at the time you were building Hearth, right? Can you tell us a bit more about what it was? Yes, Hearth AI was founded in 2022 and it was the first agentic CRM. So by 2023 we're using GPT-3.5 and we were trying to kick off all these agentic workflows when the underlying systems were still pretty fragile. We were trying to wrangle you and other people. It was super early. I remember you had great feedback for the API at the time and one of the things that you were trying to get done was like structured outputs before we even had them. You were trying to do retrieval with JSON schemas. You were so early. What was it at the time to build in AI? Yeah, so I mean my history was as an ML engineer so I had been studying and I really rode the wave of deep learning and the commercialization of deep learning. And then when agentic infrastructure and LLMs came out for the first time it was totally different, right? It was non-deterministic and it was seeing what we could stabilize within a product experience. Because our users didn't care that LLMs were new, they wanted to see the flexibility of what was just then possible. Yeah, that's amazing. Yeah, so I really try to stay within frontier tech and Stanford has a solar car team with strong ties to like Tesla and SpaceX. So for two years I was building heads down a solar-powered car with that team. I was on telemetry, CAD modeling, meshing, and then autocross. So as one of the drivers I was training within autocross clinics throughout California. That's crazy. And so did you actually race in that car that you built? So we shipped the car to Australia. Every two years Australia has an international solar car competition, the Bridgestone World Solar Challenge. And we spent three months there prepping, we were camping in the Australian outback. And then I would drive for six hours at a time through the outback and then we did the race which was five days from Darwin to Adelaide. Wow, was that scary? Yeah, we were going 90 kph which is around 60 miles an hour. And we built the car end to end so it wasn't like a company came in and did it and teams were crashing; Cambridge's team had crashed. And so I was very aware that I had spent two years building something with a team that I would put my body into. That level of rigor really played into later experiences. Right, because like that level of risk and rigor you need to have is extremely high. How do you make safe decisions and also really push the boundaries of what's technically possible? Amazing. So I view the builder archetype as quite close to the artist archetype. I think I'm happiest viewing myself as a conduit for creativity and I think engineering for me has always felt like a tool for creating and incarnating. And to be able to do it with satellites and cars and code but also poetry and videos and other mediums, that is such an energizing way to spend every day. Incredible. And so when you were so early building an AI product like three years ago, when you look back now how do you think about that experience? Like what were the hardest parts or the things you did not expect? Yeah, I think there were so many norms that were being disrupted. So the fact that our team was tearing down our agentic stack every couple of weeks, that was really hard to maybe explain to folks around us. Because the history of tech had been okay, in ML you maybe spend a year laying the data pipes and then and only then can you do something predictive and interesting with the ML product. And for us it was like no, we want to surface an experience to users that is flexible. But it was so open world that there wasn't consumer behavior yet around querying an agent. For example, folks didn't know in 2023 that in Slack the Slack bot could respond. They just thought that it was an outbound ping. So there was a lot of new behavior from the eng side but also from the user side to educate them on. And I remember on my side I was trying to build agents too before joining OpenAI and you know this was the era of nanoGPT, babyAGI, and people were trying to find the early incarnation of what an agent would be. But most people outside of the space would not really understand and grasp what an agent could do. Mhm, and I think for me it underlined the importance of having relationships with other builders, you know. We were really all in the same plane of learning this stuff for the first time and to have friends that were open and transparent where we were sharing tricks and also friends within AI labs. That really made the difference with creating products that worked in some capacity. Mhm, and so you've worked on very deep tech and very deep systems, right? What got you to think about these human connections, human relationships, which is kind of the core thesis of Hearth, right? Yeah, there is this amazing book "When Breath Becomes Air" written by Paul Kalanithi. And I didn't realize it but he had sat in on a neuroscience class that I was in and he was presented as this guest that had a terminal illness. And he was like you get to ask this person anything and you know he has this terminal illness and how is he viewing life. What he talks about is all achieving all of these accolades and making it to the top of the mountain and essentially looking around and being like "and now what." And I think from a young age I had all of this access to really interesting frontier tech but I was realizing I kind of missed that spiritual footing. And for me after some soul searching, I think like for so many people, what do I think I'll care about on my deathbed? You know, the satellites are amazing, the solar car is amazing, all the ML stuff is cool, but I think what I'm going to care about is how connected I was to myself and to other people in my life and how much presence and love I exchanged. And then it became obvious I want to push the needle on that, on connection. And I think I say this a lot but the arc of human progress bends towards love and connection and innovation and AI, I believe, should push the needle on that. Yeah, and you know even before that I had been working on teams where they had an external network of people and the task was to mine that network to match people with other people or opportunities or capital. And so it's kind of this age-old problem of as modern humans we are connected to so many more people than ever before and maybe feeling lonelier and less able to act on it both individually and as part of any cooperative team. And so I've been fixated on this for so many years and the agentic CRM was a multiplayer pass at it but, you know, my thesis is on relational intelligence. It's this belief that AI should augment the human experience and allow us to feel more connected in the time that we have and that AI should extend our brain's ability to reason on who we're connected to and why. So it will be this continuous feedback loop on our stream of consciousness in which the AI is trusted as maybe this bird's eye intelligence to guide us to see farther than we could with our own cognitive limitations. Anthropic’s $30B Ramp, Mythos Doomsday, OpenClaw Ankled, Iran War Ceasefire, Israel's Influence

All-In Podcast(1.1M) ·89:17 ·238.5K views · 2026-04-10
Brad Gerstner (Founder & CEO, Altimeter Capital) Chamath Palihapitiya (Founder & CEO, Social Capital) David Sacks (Co-founder, Craft Ventures)
Anthropic has achieved an unprecedented $30B revenue run rate while sparking intense debate over its decision to withhold its "Mythos" model due to cyber-security risks, amid accusations of anti-competitive behavior toward open-source projects like OpenClaw.
TL;DR Anthropic has achieved an unprecedented $30B revenue run rate while sparking intense debate over its decision to withhold its "Mythos" model due to cyber-security risks, amid accusations of anti-competitive behavior toward open-source projects like OpenClaw. Mythos Model...

TL;DR

Anthropic has achieved an unprecedented $30B revenue run rate while sparking intense debate over its decision to withhold its "Mythos" model due to cyber-security risks, amid accusations of anti-competitive behavior toward open-source projects like OpenClaw.

AI Security and the "Mythos" Disclosure

  • Mythos Model Security: Anthropic is withholding its latest model, Mythos, claiming it is too dangerous for public release.
      • Vulnerability Discovery: The model autonomously identified thousands of bugs, including a 27-year-old vulnerability in OpenBSD and a 16-year-old bug in FFmpeg.
      • Chaining Exploits: Dario Amodei (Anthropic CEO) reported that the model can string together four or five minor vulnerabilities to create sophisticated offensive exploits.
  • Project Glass Wing: Anthropic established a 100-day "cyber coalition" with 40 major companies (including Apple, Microsoft, and JP Morgan) to patch these vulnerabilities before hackers can exploit them.
  • Strategic Skepticism: The Besties debated whether this is genuine safety or a "marketing stunt."
      • Marketing Tactics: David Sacks noted Anthropic has a proven pattern of using "fear-mongering" to market new products, citing a previous "blackmail study" that failed to manifest in reality.
      • "Theatrical" Safety: Chamath Palihapitiya characterized the move as "theater," comparing it to the GPT-2 release in 2019, which was hyped as dangerous but turned out to be a "nothing burger."

Market Dominance and the "OpenClaw" Conflict

  • "Ankling" Open Source: Anthropic is accused of trying to stifle OpenClaw, the most popular open-source project on GitHub.
      • Pricing Shift: Anthropic moved OpenClaw users from $200/month flat-rate subscriptions to metered API usage, effectively increasing costs by 10x for power users.
      • Feature Copying: Shortly after raising costs for OpenClaw users, Anthropic released its own "safer" first-party version of the agent technology.
  • Coding Market Share: David Sacks argued that Anthropic now holds "dominant market share" in AI coding, providing over 50-60% of coding tokens.
  • Antitrust Concerns: Sacks warned that the government might eventually view these "price discrimination" tactics against open-source competitors as anti-competitive behavior.
  • The "Barbell" Approach: Brad Gerstner noted that while frontier models are winning, 65-70% of many companies' token consumption remains on open-source models for cost optimization.

Anthropic’s $30B Revenue Ramp and AGI Threshold

  • Unprecedented Growth: Anthropic’s revenue run rate hit $30B in March 2025, a ramp described as the fastest in technology history.
      • Monthly Gains: The company added $11B in annualized run rate in March alone—surpassing the combined total revenue of DataBricks and Palantir in 30 days.
      • Projection: Brad Gerstner expects Anthropic to exit the year at an $80B–$100B revenue run rate.
  • Efficiency Metrics: Anthropic reached these milestones with only 2,500 employees, compared to incumbents like Google which required 120,000+ employees to reach similar scale.
  • The TAM for Intelligence: Gerstner argued that the "TAM for intelligence" is effectively infinite and fundamentally different from previous software markets because it focuses on labor augmentation and replacement.
  • OpenAI’s Response: Despite the "vibe shift" toward Anthropic, OpenAI is preparing to release "Spud" (a Blackwell-trained model), which insiders claim is on par with Anthropic’s Mythos.

Geopolitics: Iran War and Middle East Influence

  • Ceasefire and Negotiation: A two-week ceasefire is in effect as a U.S. delegation, including JD Vance and Jared Kushner, travels to Islamabad to negotiate peace terms.
  • The "Trump Doctrine": Brad Gerstner summarized Trump’s strategy as destroying military capabilities and exiting quickly, rather than attempting "Wilsonian democracy" or nation-building.
  • Israeli Influence: The panel discussed the perception that Israeli Prime Minister Netanyahu is driving U.S. foreign policy.
      • Public Sentiment: David Sacks highlighted a "serious situation" for Israel, noting polling data from former PM Naftali Bennett showing Israel’s popularity is declining significantly in the U.S.
      • Escalation Trap: Sacks praised the ceasefire as a necessary move to prevent the war from moving further up the "escalation ladder."

The Dark Side of Sam Altman's Ambition

Hard Fork(56.1K) ·39:11 ·5.3K views · 2026-04-10
Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI) Ronan Farrow (Staff Writer, The New Yorker) Andrew Marantz (Staff Writer, The New Yorker)
Investigative reporters Ronan Farrow and Andrew Marantz detail a "forensic and even" portrait of OpenAI CEO Sam Altman, revealing a long-standing pattern of alleged manipulation, strategic deception, and a lack of institutional transparency that persists despite his public commitment to AI safety.
TL;DR Investigative reporters Ronan Farrow and Andrew Marantz detail a "forensic and even" portrait of OpenAI CEO Sam Altman, revealing a long-standing pattern of alleged manipulation, strategic deception, and a lack of institutional transparency that persists despite his public...

TL;DR

Investigative reporters Ronan Farrow and Andrew Marantz detail a "forensic and even" portrait of OpenAI CEO Sam Altman, revealing a long-standing pattern of alleged manipulation, strategic deception, and a lack of institutional transparency that persists despite his public commitment to AI safety.

The "Altman Pattern" of Deception

  • Cumulative Fact-Pattern: Farrow and Marantz argue that while there is no single "smoking gun," a massive "preponderance of evidence" suggests Altman repeatedly lies to different audiences to achieve his goals.
      • Micro-Inconsistencies: The reporters noted small but telling discrepancies, such as Altman claiming to wear the same gray sweater daily to avoid decision fatigue, only to appear in a different color for the next interview.
      • The "Rap Sheets": Internal "rap sheets" and memos documenting Altman’s behavior have circulated within the industry for years, including notes compiled by Anthropic co-founder Dario Amodei and OpenAI’s Ilya Sutskever.
  • Psychological Profile: Former associates described Altman as having an "almost sociopathic lack of concern" for the consequences of deceiving others, often navigating around the very guardrails he helped create.

Governance Failures and the "Missing" Report

  • Lack of Transparency: Following Altman's brief firing in 2023, the board promised an independent investigation. The reporters revealed that no written report was ever produced, a move likely intended to limit liability and transparency.
      • The 800-Word Summary: Instead of a detailed finding, the company released a vague press release to "rubber-stamp" Altman's return, leaving senior executives and stakeholders in the dark.
  • Strategic Board Composition: Critics allege Altman previously "stacked" his board with "JV people" (junior varsity) who were unprepared for the ruthless corporate warfare that occurred during the attempted coup.
  • Professionalization and Succession: Altman has recently hired several former public company CEOs (from Instacart, Nextdoor, and Slack) as lieutenants, which may serve to professionalize the company or create a pool of potential successors.

The "AGI Dictator" and Global Influence

  • Concentrated Power: The reporters highlighted the irony of OpenAI’s founding mission to avoid an "AGI dictatorship," noting that Altman has effectively become the singular authority over the technology.
      • Financial Ties: Altman’s personal influence is bolstered by a massive investment portfolio of approximately 400 companies and deep, previously underreported ties to Emirati and Saudi royals.
  • Regulatory Capture: Farrow noted that by seeding power to individual companies, the public is left relying on the "whims" and "quality control" of individuals rather than robust regulatory guardrails.
  • Media Strategy: OpenAI recently acquired the tech chat show *The Big Priority* (TBP), suggesting a move to control the "press infrastructure" used to tell the company's story.

Competitive Rivalries and Smear Campaigns

  • The "Mercenary" Landscape: The reporting acknowledges that Altman is the target of legitimate smear campaigns orchestrated by rivals like Elon Musk.
      • Filtering Narratives: Farrow and Marantz spent months filtering "scurrilous rumors" from evidence-based critiques, noting that Musk uses intermediaries to circulate "spicy and unsubstantiated" material.
  • The High-Stakes Race: The level of vitriol in Silicon Valley is currently "shocking and unprecedented," driven by the belief that whoever reaches AGI first will effectively control the world.

One Good Thing: Humanity and Utility

  • The Artemis II Mission: Kevin Roose highlighted NASA’s upcoming lunar mission, noting its capacity to inspire "childlike wonder" and its achievement in passing the Bechdel test through communication between female specialists and controllers.
  • Acme Weather: Casey Newton recommended a new app from the founders of Dark Sky.
      • Advanced Features: The app provides "Bayesian" probability charts for temperature, sunset beauty alerts, and community-sourced "rainbow notifications."

TSM, ASML And Goldman Sachs Lead Earnings Amid Iran War Volatility | IBD

Investor's Business Daily(174.0K) ·28:10 ·1.3K views · 2026-04-10
Alexis Garcia (Host, Investor's Business Daily) Ed Carson (Reporter, Investor's Business Daily)
The upcoming earnings reports from Taiwan Semiconductor, ASML, and Goldman Sachs will serve as a critical litmus test for the sustainability of the AI boom and the health of capital markets amidst heightened geopolitical volatility following recent Iranian military activity.
TL;DR The upcoming earnings reports from Taiwan Semiconductor, ASML, and Goldman Sachs will serve as a critical litmus test for the sustainability of the AI boom and the health of capital markets amidst heightened geopolitical volatility following recent Iranian military...

TL;DR

The upcoming earnings reports from Taiwan Semiconductor, ASML, and Goldman Sachs will serve as a critical litmus test for the sustainability of the AI boom and the health of capital markets amidst heightened geopolitical volatility following recent Iranian military activity.

Geopolitical Volatility & Forward Guidance

  • Setting the Scene: Ed Carson notes that while Q1 results may be strong, they largely reflect a period before the current Iran-Israel tensions.
  • Commentary Over Results: Corporate guidance and conference call tones will be more significant than historical numbers as investors look for signs of caution regarding oil prices and economic stability.
  • The "I Don’t Know" Factor: Executives are expected to be non-committal about long-term impacts, but their level of concern regarding inflation and energy costs will dictate market sentiment.

Semiconductor Bellwethers: TSM & ASML

  • Taiwan Semiconductor (TSM): Reporting Thursday, April 16.
      • Growth Targets: Projected EPS of $3.26 (+52%) and revenue of $35.2 billion (+26%).
      • CapEx Indicator: Investors are focusing on the massive capital expenditure guidance (raised to $52B–$56B) as a signal for AI demand through 2027–2028.
      • Manufacturing Dominance: As the primary manufacturer for Nvidia and Broadcom, TSM remains the ultimate proxy for the chip industry.
  • ASML: Reporting Wednesday, April 15.
      • Backlog Focus: Entered 2024 with a record backlog; net bookings will be the primary indicator of future growth.
      • China Restrictions: Commentary on the MATCH Act and lithography gear export restrictions to China remains a key regulatory headwind.
      • EPS/Revenue: Expected EPS of $7.70 (+13%) on revenue of $10.08 billion (+14%).

Banking & Capital Markets

  • Goldman Sachs (GS): Reporting Monday, April 13.
      • IPO Rebound: Goldman’s heavy reliance on investment banking fees makes the IPO pipeline (including a potential SpaceX filing) the primary catalyst for a revenue rebound.
      • Financial Metrics: Projected EPS of $16.35 (+16%) and revenue of $16.95 billion (+13%).
      • Wealth Management Strategy: Focus remains on building durable revenue streams via asset management to offset the volatility of capital markets.
  • The Broader Banking Sector:
      • JPMorgan (JPM): Viewed as a broader economic bellwether due to its retail exposure, credit card data, and lending trends.
      • Citigroup (C): Currently showing strong technical performance with its Relative Strength (RS) line hitting new highs ahead of its report.

Technical Analysis & Chart Patterns

  • Sector Rotation: Ed Carson advises looking for leading stocks outside of the AI/Hardware space—such as financials—to avoid over-concentration if tech valuations roll over.
  • Chart Levels to Watch:
      • Goldman Sachs: Trading in a consolidation pattern with a $984.70 buy point.
      • TSM: Testing its 21-day and 50-day moving averages; ideally needs to form a "handle" on an emerging cup base.
      • Netflix (NFLX): Though a fundamental leader, the chart shows "overhead resistance," and analysts suggest waiting for a new base above the 200-day line before entry.

Trump Demands Reopening of Hormuz Ahead of US-Iran Talks | The Opening Trade 4/10/2026

Bloomberg Television(3.1M) ·72:10 ·4.3K views · 2026-04-10
Donald Trump (President of the United States)
Global markets are cautiously pricing in a positive outcome for high-stakes peace talks between the U.S.
TL;DR Global markets are cautiously pricing in a positive outcome for high-stakes peace talks between the U.S. and Iran in Islamabad this weekend, even as the Strait of Hormuz remains closed and critical Saudi energy infrastructure faces significant damage. High-Stakes Peace...

TL;DR

Global markets are cautiously pricing in a positive outcome for high-stakes peace talks between the U.S. and Iran in Islamabad this weekend, even as the Strait of Hormuz remains closed and critical Saudi energy infrastructure faces significant damage.

Geopolitical Negotiations: The Islamabad Summit

  • High-Stakes Peace Talks: U.S. and Iranian officials are convening in Pakistan to turn a fragile ceasefire into a permanent settlement.
      • U.S. Delegation: Represented by Vice President JD Vance, Steve Witkoff, and Jared Kushner.
      • Iranian Delegation: Led by Speaker of the Parliament Mohammad Ghalibaf and Foreign Minister Abbas Araghchi.
  • Core Points of Contention: The sides remain far apart on fundamental issues.
      • U.S. Demands: Zero nuclear enrichment, dismantled nuclear sites, limited ballistic capabilities, and an unconditional reopening of the Strait of Hormuz.
      • Iranian Demands: Sovereignty over the Strait (including potential toll collection), war reparations, and continued support for regional proxies.
  • The Israel-Lebanon Track: In a separate development, Prime Minister Netanyahu has agreed to direct talks with the Lebanese government in D.C. next week, specifically focused on disarming Hezbollah.

Energy Markets: The Hormuz Bottleneck

  • Strait of Hormuz Status: Despite the ceasefire, the Strait remains effectively closed.
      • Shipping Gridlock: Approximately 800 ships remain stuck in the Persian Gulf. While some Chinese and Japanese tankers are inching closer, owners and insurers remain hesitant due to reports that the waterway is mined.
      • De-mining Timeline: Bloomberg Intelligence suggests that clearing the Strait of mines could be a "months-long process."
  • Saudi Infrastructure Damage: Saudi Arabia confirmed a production capacity cut of 600,000 barrels per day (bpd) due to attacks. Additionally, the East-West pipeline saw throughput reduced by 700,000 bpd.
  • Price Divergence: While Brent futures have seen a weekly loss, physical "prompt" Brent (for immediate delivery) remains extremely high at approximately $130 per barrel.
  • Domestic Impact: In India, major providers like Reliance are already rationing fuel, limiting customers to roughly $11 worth of petrol or diesel per visit.

Economic Outlook and Market Volatility

  • U.S. Inflation Concerns: Investors are bracing for a U.S. CPI print expected to jump to 3.4% (up from 2.4% the previous month), largely driven by war-related energy costs.
  • Market Complacency: Beata Manthey (Citi Head of European Equity Strategy) warns that the current risk rally is driven by "short covering" rather than fundamental strength. She notes that the market is currently "priced for upgrades," which may be unrealistic given deteriorating labor markets and exhausted consumer savings compared to 2022.
  • China’s Pivot: China has moved out of a three-year deflationary period in producer prices, largely due to rising commodity costs (oil and metals), potentially reducing the need for state stimulus.
  • The "Barbell" Strategy: Camila Astaburuaga (EFG Asset Management) recommends a barbell approach—holding higher-beta short-end assets for carry, hedged with high-quality long-duration bonds to protect against a "growth shock" if the war persists.

Technology and Cybersecurity Risks

  • AI Resilience: TSMC reported a sales beat, suggesting that AI demand remains insulated from geopolitical shocks. This has provided a lift to European semiconductor equipment makers like ASML.
  • The "Mythos" Threat: U.S. Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent and Fed Chair Jerome Powell summoned Wall Street leaders to discuss Anthropic’s new "Mythos" AI model.
      • Cyber Vulnerabilities: The system reportedly identified software bugs dating back 30 years within hours—vulnerabilities that human experts had missed.
      • Regulatory Fear: Concerns center on the model's ability to exploit every major operating system if used offensively.

Notable Quotes

"The European market is still priced for upgrades, and that is a problem... we are not going back to the world the market has been putting bets on at the start of the year."

— Beata Manthey, Head of European Equity Strategy at Citi

"Iran is maintaining that they should be allowed to enrich uranium for civilian purposes... and they are also reiterating their desire to maintain some sort of sovereignty over the Strait of Hormuz. Clearly a non-starter there."

— Joumanna Bercetche, Bloomberg Correspondent

"Hormuz is closed... while this ceasefire is supposed to mean an opening of the Strait, when you look at the actual traffic, it’s just ships doing a whole lot of nothing."

— Stephen Stapczynski, Bloomberg Asia Energy Service Leader

"President Trump says he is very optimistic about a deal, but later threatened Tehran over charging fees in the Strait of Hormuz."

— Anna Edwards, Bloomberg Anchor

How Prediction Markets Turned the World Into a Casino

Bloomberg Originals(5.0M) ·12:40 ·111.2K views · 2026-04-10
Prediction markets like Kalshi and Polymarket have transitioned from niche gambling sites to multi-billion-dollar financial platforms, gaining legitimacy through mainstream partnerships and political support while facing intense legal scrutiny over state tax revenues and ethical concerns.
TL;DR Prediction markets like Kalshi and Polymarket have transitioned from niche gambling sites to multi-billion-dollar financial platforms, gaining legitimacy through mainstream partnerships and political support while facing intense legal scrutiny over state tax revenues and...

TL;DR

Prediction markets like Kalshi and Polymarket have transitioned from niche gambling sites to multi-billion-dollar financial platforms, gaining legitimacy through mainstream partnerships and political support while facing intense legal scrutiny over state tax revenues and ethical concerns.

Rapid Growth and Institutional Mainstreaming

  • Exponential Volume Increase: Kalshi’s notional trading volume surged from $30 million per week at the start of 2024 to approximately $3 billion per week by early 2025.
  • Corporate Integration:
      • Tech & Finance: Google is integrating market data into its finance AI tools, and the owner of the New York Stock Exchange has taken a stake in Polymarket.
      • Media & Sports: CNN and CNBC have partnered with Kalshi, while Major League Baseball (MLB) named Polymarket its official prediction market partner.
  • Political Alignment: The Trump administration has taken a pro-industry stance, with Donald Trump Jr. serving as an advisor to Kalshi and an investor in Polymarket. Trump Media has also announced its own platform, "Truth Predict."

Regulatory Conflict and Legal Challenges

  • Derivative vs. Gambling: Platforms argue they trade peer-to-peer (P2P) derivatives regulated by the Commodity Futures Trading Commission (CFTC), rather than acting as a traditional "house" that bets against users.
  • State vs. Federal Jurisdiction:
      • Tax Revenue: States that have legalized sports betting are suing platforms because they cannot collect tax revenue on Kalshi or Polymarket trades.
      • Criminal Charges: Arizona has filed criminal charges against Kalshi, alleging the exchange operates an illegal gambling business.
  • Election Precedent: The 2024 U.S. Presidential election served as a proof-of-concept; prediction markets were generally more accurate than traditional polling, significantly boosting the industry's reputation.

Ethical Concerns and Market Mechanics

  • Insider Trading: Critics argue these markets are easily gamed, but some proponents view insider trading as a "feature" rather than a bug, as it incentivizes the disclosure of non-public information to create more accurate market prices.
      • Example: An anonymous trader on Polymarket reportedly made $400,000 betting on the capture of Nicolas Maduro, suggesting advanced knowledge of the event.
  • The "Financialization of Everything": Markets allow users to wager on sensitive topics such as military conflicts (US-Israel-Iran war), assassinations, and natural disasters.
      • Platform Differences: While Kalshi adheres to CFTC rules banning bets on war or terrorism, the international platform Polymarket hosts many of these controversial "esoteric" markets.
  • Liquidity Risks: Many niche markets (e.g., specific pop culture events) have low volume, making them susceptible to price manipulation by individuals with relatively small amounts of capital.

Retail Impact and "Dumb Money"

  • Demographics: Success stories exist—such as users paying off student loans or cars—but the majority of retail investors, particularly males aged 18–35, are losing money to professional "finance bros."
  • Market Divergence:
      • Professional Side: Sophisticated traders use high-level research and post-notifications to gain an edge on "dumb money" (casual users).
      • Utility: While platforms offer bets on interest rates and economic data, approximately 70% of Kalshi's weekly volume is currently driven by sports-related wagers.
"In the original premise of prediction market, an insider information can be a signal... insider trading is not necessarily a bug that needs to be fixed, but it's very much a feature."

— Transcript Narrator/Analyst

"I am not a finance bro, I have no background on the stock market... I go on Twitter and I follow fan pages... and I turn on post notifications, so super easy. Anyone can do it."

— Brandon Fean, 25-year-old teacher and Polymarket trader

"To those who seek to challenge our authority in this space, let me be clear: we will see you in court."

— CFTC Representative (under the Trump Administration)

This year's richest person, the existence of aliens, even the return of Jesus Christ. Believe it or not, people are actually betting on stuff like this. Prediction markets are platforms online where you can bet on future events. The best known platforms are Kalshi and Polymarket, but there are a bunch of others. And over the past year, they've exploded in popularity. Kalshi partnered with CNN and CNBC. Google announced it will integrate Kalshi and Polymarket predictions into its finance AI tools. MLB even named Polymarket its official prediction market partner. We have the New York Stock Exchange owner taking a stake into Polymarket. You're starting to see Polymarket and Kalshi really get into the mainstream vernacular. If you had me saying "potato" on Polymarket, you just made a ton of money. Prediction market app dude, you know, online peer-to-peer betting. The amount of money involved has skyrocketed. Kalshi currently handles three billion of notional trading a week. Just at the beginning of 2025, they were handling 30 million, so it is a hundred times more. We're seeing pretty similar numbers for Polymarket. The boom is creating a new class of market watchers. You're just placing trades based on the news and the information that you have. Information is power. I was able to pay off my student loans with the money I got from Kalshi. I'm putting myself through grad school, and I was able to pay off my car. But most people aren't paying off their bills; they're losing money. I feel so bad for 18 to 35-year-old males losing so much money. People are gambling on war. As the US and Israel prepared to strike Iran, platforms like Polymarket saw a surge in activity. One of the criticisms of prediction markets is that they've created the financialization of everything. Should you be even allowed to bet on military endeavors, or should that be something that is off-limits? As these markets get more esoteric and specific, they become more easily gameable and more difficult to monitor for insider trading and market manipulation. So what happens when you can bet on almost anything? From the perspective of the prediction markets, this is not gambling. This is trading a derivative like any other derivative, whether it's wheat futures or something that has real-world utility. Kalshi or any other prediction market is regulated by the Commodity Futures Trading Commission on a federal level. It's a yes or a no outcome. When you buy shares, they can be priced anywhere from one cent to ninety-nine cents. That price is determined by what traders are willing to pay for that outcome at that moment in time. Unlike sports gambling, prediction markets don't have a house taking the opposite side of your bet. We don't actually make money when people lose. Instead, they match buyers and sellers and generate revenue from transaction fees. Prediction markets are based on supply and demand. It's a peer-to-peer market. If I place a "yes" on a certain outcome, whether it's going to rain in Central Park tomorrow, there needs to be enough people thinking that it's not going to rain to take the opposite side of the bet. And while you're welcome to make predictions about the weather, most people on these platforms are there for sports. Indiana's gonna win, baby! As of March, around 70 percent of Kalshi's weekly trading volume was entirely made up of sports-related bets. Sports gambling was mostly illegal in the US until 2018 when the Supreme Court struck down a federal ban. Today, nearly 40 states have legalized it in some form, bringing in billions of dollars in tax revenue each year. The states that have legalized sports betting are upset because they can't control the sports betting that's going on on Kalshi and, crucially, they can't collect tax revenues on it. Now, other states that have not legalized sports betting are upset because now, without their consent or their control, sports betting is now legal. Several US states have sued Kalshi, Polymarket, Robinhood, and a bunch of other platforms, saying that they should have jurisdiction over what they consider to be sports gambling. From the consumer's perspective, even though the underlying plumbing is different and the regulations are somewhat different, when you open the app, it's very much the same experience. Arizona is filing criminal charges against Kalshi, accusing the prediction market exchange of operating an illegal gambling business. We see this as a total overstep and we look forward to fighting it in court. But most retail investors, regular people on the street, they're probably less interested in betting on what interest rates will be next month, but they're pretty interested in betting on who'll win the Super Bowl. Billions are also expected to be placed on World Cup outcomes. 93 percent on Kalshi right now. Yes. This is how we bring the numbers down. People also like to bet on politics. Polymarket and Kalshi particularly got big around the 2024 US presidential election. So look, this election is going to be close, but we are going to win. Everyone was talking about the odds of President Trump winning the election. And there was somewhat of a gap. Those markets actually predicted that Trump would win more accurately than polls did. So that helped improve the reputation of prediction markets. Trump's victory was a win for prediction markets as well. Donald Trump Jr. is an advisor to Kalshi and he's both an advisor and an investor in Polymarket. The Trump Media company has its own prediction market platform that it's announced called Truth Predict. The Biden administration had been critical and went after Polymarket and Kalshi with mixed results. But the same agency under Trump has come out swinging for the industry to keep it legal. To those who seek to challenge our authority in this space, let me be clear: we will see you in court. At the end of the day, there's a limit to regulatory control. Polymarket has since become federally registered and is offering some bets in the US again, even while most people can access the full service via Virtual Private Networks or VPNs. Conversely, Kalshi is trying to expand overseas. For Kalshi, they've said they want to be available in 140 different countries. That's the goal. But a cloud of ethical questions linger. Take insider trading. In investing, there are hard rules around who and how information can be used, but here it's fuzzy. In the original premise of the prediction market, insider information can be a signal. I think what's cool about Polymarket is that it creates this financial incentive for people to go and divulge information to the market and the market to change. What we often see in these prediction markets is that insider trading is not necessarily a bug that needs to be fixed, but it's very much a feature. From this point of view, people with insider information can profit from it. The most famous example is someone in the run-up to the US capturing Nicolas Maduro who placed a series of large bets that Maduro would be removed from office. They ended up making $400,000 off of these bets. That was on Polymarket, so those bets were anonymous, but it appears to be someone who had knowledge that this was going to happen. In some cases, bets don't have to be big to make a difference. There are so many things to bet on and some of them don't have a lot of volume or a lot of liquidity. It doesn't take a huge amount of money to move those markets. Polymarket has introduced rules that it says are aimed at curbing trading on non-public information. And Kalshi says it will penalize such behavior or refer it to the CFTC. This evolving set of rules doesn't completely squash the ability to stack odds and profit from insider information, though. And there's also the dubious ethics of being able to bet on conflict or violence. On Kalshi, you're not allowed to bet on assassination, terrorism, or war because the CFTC has those outlined in its core principles as areas where bets are not permitted. But for Polymarket, those rules don't apply because it's international, so it has a lot of markets on areas that people would consider unethical. Another issue is how outcomes are decided. Kalshi and Polymarket lay out for bettors what event resolution criteria and data sources will determine the answer to the wager. In theory, who wins and who loses should be decided by the real world. That said, there can be hiccups when these markets resolve. Despite the issues, Kalshi and Polymarket are raising billions of dollars in funding. The boom in online prediction markets is being driven by people like Brandon Fean, a 25-year-old teacher in Pennsylvania. I am not a finance bro; I have no background on the stock market. My friend actually reached out to me and said that there was a way to bet on the Billboard Hot 100 chart and try to predict what song was going to be number one. And I went, "There is no way that that is a real thing," because that is what I have done since I was in eighth grade. My first trade that I did, I lost $600 and I was like, "You know what, I'm all done." But Fean didn't quit. Instead, he approached it like a professional investor would, with research. Sometimes it'll be hours each day, sometimes it'll be one hour for the week. I have seen success with Taylor Swift markets with her album, how many sales she was going to do. I go on Twitter and I follow fan pages that update on these things and I turn on post notifications, so it's super easy. Anyone can do it; anyone can look at how people are finding this information. Here's how you can make a few thousand dollars on Polymarket. This is the number one strategy to start making money on Polymarket today. This poly was... a lot of people are taking this very seriously and doing a lot of research and trying to get an edge on what they call dumb money. These are people who are just kind of winging it. And in some cases, the kind of research they were doing was incredibly sophisticated. A lot of the biggest supporters say, wow, this will be bigger than the stock market; this will be bigger than all financial assets because it allows you to do everything. And perhaps that's part of the promise. But then the other side of the coin is that the majority of people go for entertainment. They go to win some money; they go to watch a game. Be it entertainment or investing, prediction markets tend to produce big winners and lots of losers. Analysis done by Bloomberg and other outlets shows that on average, bettors who invest less money in prediction markets tend to lose more often and those who invest more money, you know, the professional side, they tend to win bigger. That tendency sounds a lot like Wall Street, where big investors tend to make most of the money. But as with anything this new, everything is subject to change. It's possible that a lot of this could go away. We're also waiting to see how a lot of these court cases shake out. If the courts decide that trading on prediction markets is the same as sports betting and should be regulated by the states, then that completely changes these companies' business models.

Wall Street CEOs Summoned to Discuss Anthropic AI Risks | Bloomberg Tech

Bloomberg Podcasts(463.0K) ·43:48 ·346 views · 2026-04-10
Caroline Hyde (Anchor, Bloomberg Television)
Top U.S.
TL;DR Top U.S. financial regulators held an urgent, undisclosed meeting with Wall Street CEOs to address systemic cyber risks posed by Anthropic’s advanced "Mythos" AI model, amid a broader market shift where hardware infrastructure providers are surging while software...

TL;DR

Top U.S. financial regulators held an urgent, undisclosed meeting with Wall Street CEOs to address systemic cyber risks posed by Anthropic’s advanced "Mythos" AI model, amid a broader market shift where hardware infrastructure providers are surging while software valuations face significant pressure.

Wall Street Summit on AI Cyber Risk

  • Urgent Regulatory Intervention: Treasury Secretary Scott Besant and Fed Chair Jerome Powell summoned leaders from Goldman Sachs, Citigroup, and Bank of America to discuss potential vulnerabilities introduced by Anthropic’s new "Mythos" AI model.
      • Project Glass Wing: A restricted testing phase involving 40 partners, including JPMorgan, designed to stress-test the model’s impact on financial systems.
      • Systemic Concerns: Discussion focused on protecting internal consumer data and preventing the technology from identifying and exploiting vulnerabilities in systemically important financial institutions.
  • The "Hush-Hush" Nature: Katherine Doherty (Bloomberg Banking Reporter) noted the meeting was added to the schedule last minute and kept quiet to avoid public alarm, signaling the high level of concern regarding AI’s potential to facilitate cyber-attacks.

The Infrastructure Boom: CoreWeave and the "Luxury" AI Trade

  • Major Contract Wins: CoreWeave shares rose 13% following a multi-year deal with Anthropic, closely following a $21 billion agreement with Meta.
      • Financial Strength: The company reports a contracted backlog of approximately $90 billion with five-year minimum commitments from "A-list" talent like Microsoft and Google.
      • "Luxury" Builder Label: Brent Thill (Jefferies Analyst) described CoreWeave as the "luxury AI builder to the stars," noting that even hyperscalers like Microsoft lack the physical data center capacity to meet current demand.
  • Capital Strategy: Michael Intrator (CoreWeave CEO) defended the company’s use of convertible debt and high-yield bonds, stating that the quality of their counterparties (like Meta) allows them to lower their cost of capital significantly.
      • Global Expansion: Despite reports of OpenAI pulling back from UK projects due to energy costs, CoreWeave intends to continue its UK expansion, citing strong relationships with local energy providers.

Market Bifurcation: Hardware Surge vs. Software Slump

  • Hardware Resilience: TSMC reported a 35% revenue increase, defying Middle East geopolitical volatility. This signals that AI infrastructure demand remains decoupled from broader macroeconomic concerns.
  • Software Sell-off: Software stocks are struggling globally. Brent Thill noted that software is going "straight down" while hardware goes "straight up" because the immediate ROI on software remains unproven while the "picks and shovels" are essential today.
      • Specific Losers: Palo Alto Networks saw its worst day in two years (down 7%) as investors worry about the reconfiguration of security software in the AI era.
  • Defense and Thematic Investing: Sylvia Jablonski (Defiance ETFs CIO) highlighted a rotation into "AI War" themes, focusing on drones (Kratos, AeroVironment) and space connectivity rather than traditional software.

Aerospace Milestones and Space Economics

  • Artemis II Splashdown: The crew of Artemis II is returning from a record-breaking lunar trip.
      • Orion Heat Shield Test: The mission serves as a critical test of the Lockheed Martin-built capsule’s heat shield, which must withstand 5,000 degrees Fahrenheit—roughly the temperature of the sun's surface—during re-entry at 25,000 mph.
  • NASA’s Commercial Shift: Laurie Leshin (Professor/Former JPL Director) noted that the program is moving faster toward a 2028 "boots on the moon" goal, aided by a more streamlined, less bureaucratic approach under current leadership.

Notable Quotes

"They [CoreWeave] are the luxury AI builder to the stars... Microsoft said in their last earnings call they don't have enough physical buildings to put the compute in... Software's going straight down because CAPEX continues to explode." — Brent Thill, Analyst, Jefferies
"This is the first large-scale combat operation that was really driven, enhanced, and made substantially more productive with technology with AI." — Palantir CTO (quoted by Caroline Hyde)
"The heat shield gets to about the temperature of the surface of the sun, about 5,000 degrees Fahrenheit... This is one of the biggest challenges of space flight." — Laurie Leshin, Professor of Space Futures, Arizona State University
"Our job in this ecosystem is to provide as much infrastructure at the highest quality that we can... [Anthropic] engaged us because they have demand for compute because of the wonderful products they are bringing to market." — Michael Intrator, CEO, CoreWeave

BREAKING NEWS: Glenn Ivey Calls For Elon Musk To Be Subpoenaed, Vows To Hold ICE Accountable

Forbes Breaking News(5.5M) ·10:18 ·1.3K views · 2026-04-10
Glenn Ivey (U.S. Representative (D-MD))
Rep.
TL;DR Rep. Glenn Ivey (D-MD) announced plans to prioritize the George Floyd Justice in Policing Act and issue a primary subpoena for Elon Musk should Democrats regain the House majority. His agenda emphasizes holding ICE accountable for racial profiling, modernizing voter...

TL;DR

Rep. Glenn Ivey (D-MD) announced plans to prioritize the George Floyd Justice in Policing Act and issue a primary subpoena for Elon Musk should Democrats regain the House majority. His agenda emphasizes holding ICE accountable for racial profiling, modernizing voter outreach for younger generations, and investigating the perceived "sale" of presidential pardons.

Police Reform and the George Floyd Justice in Policing Act

  • Legislative Leadership: Rep. Ivey has taken the lead on reintroducing the George Floyd Justice in Policing Act in the House, a role previously held by the late Rep. Sheila Jackson Lee and supported by House Minority Leader Hakeem Jeffries.
  • Accountability in Minnesota: Ivey highlighted recent police-involved shootings in Minnesota, such as the cases of Rene Good and Alex Pritty.
      • State vs. Federal Prosecution: He advocated for prosecuting police misconduct in state courts rather than federal courts.
      • Strategic Reasoning: State-level convictions prevent defendants from receiving presidential pardons, citing Derek Chauvin as a primary example.
  • Evidence Transparency: Ivey initiated a letter signed by 58 colleagues demanding the Department of Justice release withheld evidence to state-level prosecutors like Keith Ellison.

ICE Oversight and Election Integrity

  • Agency Reform: Ivey called for a "total revamp" of U.S. Immigration and Customs Enforcement (ICE).
      • Mandatory Requirements: He demanded the implementation of body cameras, the removal of masks for officers, and an end to roving patrols and racial profiling.
  • Polling Place Security: There is significant concern regarding the potential presence of ICE or the National Guard at polling locations during the upcoming election.
      • Watchdog Strategy: Ivey urged the "Divine Nine" (Black Greek Letter Organizations) and the faith community to serve as extra-involved poll watchers to counter potential voter intimidation from both "MAGA" activists and federal agencies.

Modernizing Voter Outreach

  • Failure of Traditional Media: Traditional political advertising—including TV commercials, radio spots, and newspaper ads—is failing to reach voters under 30.
  • The "Trusted Voice" Model: Outreach must transition from top-down media buys to person-to-person engagement.
      • Target Platforms: Move away from Facebook toward platforms currently used by younger demographics.
      • Direct Engagement: Utilizing family networks (grandmothers, cousins) as "trusted voices" is essential for effective mobilization before the final weeks of October.

Economic Critique and Investigative Oversight

  • Cost of Living: Ivey attributed high gas and grocery prices to the previous administration's tariffs and the blocking of "green power" initiatives.
      • Energy Policy: He claimed the previous administration blocked the roll-out of green energy to protect billionaire interests, which in turn kept electricity bills high.
  • Congressional Subpoenas: If Democrats retake the House, Ivey identified several immediate investigative priorities:
      • Elon Musk: Named as the intended recipient of the first subpoena issued.
      • Asset Oversight: Investigations into a $400 million airplane deal and $4 billion in cryptocurrency transfers.
  • Pardon System Reform: Ivey criticized the use of presidential pardons for January 6th defendants and fraud cases.
      • Restitution Issues: He noted that pardoned individuals often avoid paying court-ordered restitution to victims.
      • Double Standards: Ivey highlighted a disparity in the justice system, arguing that a group of Black protesters would never have received the same leniency or pardons given to those who raided the Capitol.

Sam Altman’s Trust Issues at OpenAI | The New Yorker Radio Hour

The New Yorker(1.1M) ·44:23 ·248 views · 2026-04-10
Ronan Farrow (Journalist, The New Yorker) Andrew Marantz (Staff Writer, The New Yorker) Sam Altman (CEO, OpenAI)
Sam Altman is characterized as a "Michael Jordan of listening" whose skill in reflecting what others want to hear has driven OpenAI’s success but created a deep "trust crisis" regarding his transparency and long-term commitment to safety.
TL;DR Sam Altman is characterized as a "Michael Jordan of listening" whose skill in reflecting what others want to hear has driven OpenAI’s success but created a deep "trust crisis" regarding his transparency and long-term commitment to safety. As OpenAI shifts from a...

TL;DR

Sam Altman is characterized as a "Michael Jordan of listening" whose skill in reflecting what others want to hear has driven OpenAI’s success but created a deep "trust crisis" regarding his transparency and long-term commitment to safety. As OpenAI shifts from a safety-first nonprofit to a profit-driven geopolitical powerhouse, concerns persist that Altman’s pattern of deception makes him an unreliable steward for a technology with existential stakes.

The "Oppenheimer" Paradox and Shifting Mission

  • The Manhattan Project Analogy: Altman frequently compares AI development to the Manhattan Project, positioning himself as a modern-day Robert Oppenheimer.
      • This dual-edged rhetoric framing OpenAI as the "good guys" was used to recruit top talent (like Ilya Sutskever) by promising a focus on safety over profit.
  • From Nonprofit to For-Profit: Originally founded as a nonprofit to prevent a "rogue AI" scenario, OpenAI has transitioned into a functional for-profit entity (currently valued at approximately $122 billion).
      • Critics argue the original "safety-first" mission was a brilliant recruitment and regulatory sales pitch that has since been discarded in a "race to the bottom" for market dominance.
  • Capturing the "Light Cone": Altman’s optimistic projections involve "capturing the light cone of all economic value," a sci-fi concept suggesting the capture of all economic activity in the solar system through superabundance and AGI.

Internal Trust Issues and the 2023 "Coup"

  • The "Not Consistently Candid" Label: The board’s famous reason for firing Altman in 2023—that he was not "consistently candid"—is described by sources as a polite way of calling him a "pathological liar."
  • The Ilya Sutskever Memos: While no single "smoking gun" exists for Altman’s brief firing, a year-long investigation revealed an accumulation of deceptive patterns.
      • Example: A secret plot was allegedly entertained to pit the U.S., Russia, and China against each other by selling or giving AGI to the highest international bidder.
  • Personal Deception: Sources cite instances of Altman claiming to be a champion ping-pong player only to be the worst in the office, illustrating a habit of "banal deception" that extends to serious business and safety matters.

Geopolitical and Financial Stakes

  • Middle Eastern Funding: To sustain OpenAI’s massive cash burn, Altman is seeking astronomical sums from the Middle East for data centers.
      • One proposed data center in the UAE would be seven times the size of Central Park and consume as much electricity as the city of Miami.
  • Military Expansion: OpenAI recently pivoted to pursue Pentagon contracts after rivals (like Anthropic) refused on ethical grounds regarding autonomous weapons and surveillance.
      • While Altman publicly supported Anthropic’s "bright lines," he was privately negotiating for the same military contracts.
  • National Security Concerns: Critics fear that Altman's "nakedly transactional" approach could concentrate the world’s most powerful technology in the hands of autocracies.

Redefining AI Risk and Safety

  • The Alignment Shift: Altman has pivoted from warning that AI might "stamp out humanity" to redefining "alignment issues" as minor nuisances, similar to annoying social media algorithms.
  • Immediate Harms: Journalists highlight that AI is already being used in bioweapon development and battlefield applications where weapons can fire without human operators.
  • The Turing Test: Recent research suggests Chat-GPT is now more successful at deceiving human observers into thinking it is human than actual humans are.
  • Psychological Impact: There are ongoing lawsuits regarding AI-prompted suicides and "psychosis" driven by addiction to sycophantic chatbots.

Notable Quotes

"I don't think Sam is the guy who should have his finger on the button." — Ilya Sutskever (former OpenAI Chief Scientist, regarding Altman's leadership)
"He is the Michael Jordan of listening. He looks you in the eye and reflects back what you want to hear... He can effortlessly shift between one version of reality and another." — Ronan Farrow (Staff Writer, *The New Yorker*)
"One person close to him told us... he really seems to lack any self-doubt. That is a superpower. He believes it when he says it." — Ronan Farrow
"Actually, please regulate me because the thing I'm building is so scary that if you don't regulate me, everyone you love will die. It turns out to be a counter-intuitively very good sales pitch." — Andrew Marantz (Staff Writer, *The New Yorker*)
Now Andrew, Ronan, you compare Sam Altman to Robert Oppenheimer, who of course was pivotal in developing the A-bomb. Oppenheimer not only developed the technology but, in a sense, he defined an age in American life—the Atomic Age. But there's of course something extremely ominous about that comparison, too. So let's begin this way: Andrew, who is Sam Altman and why would you compare him to Robert Oppenheimer? Well, for one thing, we compare him to Oppenheimer because he compares himself to Oppenheimer. I mean, constantly throughout the rhetoric before OpenAI existed for why it needed to exist, there's this constant thread of analogies to the Manhattan Project. So when he emails Elon Musk out of the blue in May of 2015, he says, "Hi Elon, this is Sam, you know, we met at YC such and such." He says, "I think we need a Manhattan Project for AI." And it does have this dual-edged nature to it, which is both, "we're going to be the good guys and defeat the bad guys," right? So we're the Americans who are going to defeat the Nazis. Yeah, but so in this case, instead of the Nazis, it's either China in a national security context or Google in a competitive corporate context. But there's also the ominous part, Ronan. The notion that the atomic bomb defined an age—the Atomic Age—it still looms over our politics and global security. What is the potential ominous aspect of AI? We hear about it as something that could be fantastic for the development of drugs, for all kinds of things, but it could also wipe out God knows how many jobs. But it goes darker than that. I will say at the outset of this reporting, I was not myself convinced of the, you know, much-ballyhooed transformative impact of this technology. I really emerge from this more convinced. There's the scenarios that I think you're alluding to, right—the atom bomb-esque ones. The idea that this really could lead to a kind of Terminator Skynet scenario, where a rogue artificial intelligence falls out of alignment with what we want and takes control of nukes. But you don't have to buy into those extreme scenarios to also see the immediate impact that the entire US economy is now propped up by a few companies that are all in on AI, with OpenAI at the center of it. Most credible projections very immediately foresee millions of jobs being exposed to disruption by this. It's already taking form as a significant change agent in the way weapons are used in battlefields, that they can now just fire without a human operator. That is something in the very immediate future. It's taking form in bioweapon development. These are all things that are happening or on the verge of happening, not far-off fantasies. Now just to be clear, Andrew, what most of us have in mind in terms of AI is chatbots, which run on large language models. How is that different from the aspiration of an AGI—artificial general intelligence—something that does not yet exist? ChatGPT is a useful tool that can help you write emails, and AGI is supposed to be able to do any cognitive task that any person can do, arguably better. So it's general because it can not only write emails or play chess, but it can also discover new drugs or solve new medical problems or break new ground in physics. So we're not quite there yet. Or replace the entire staff of The New Yorker? Not quite there yet either. Won't be long, David. What's interesting, Andrew, is that you quickly describe Sam Altman as somebody who really doesn't have that much computer science knowledge. That when it comes to the actual science, the actual technology, Sam Altman is no great genius. So what does he bring to the table that makes him such a transformative figure? He is, in a sense, you know, what every entrepreneur does—is, you know, bring together technical talent and investors and merge them into a solvent company. So in that sense, it's not that remarkable. I think what makes him unusual is he's not bringing this kind of bull in a china shop—you know, he's not Elon Musk or Jeff Bezos and just saying, "I'm going to bulldoze my way to power." He brings this kind of subtle sales pitch around 2014, 2015, 2016, where the public is feeling kind of burned out on the bluster of tech tycoons. You know, everyone's suddenly mad at social media, Republicans for stifling their speech and Democrats for giving us Trump and whatever. And he comes in and, instead of saying, "Who needs Luddite regulators?" he says, "Actually, please regulate me, because the thing I'm building is so scary that if you don't regulate me, everyone you love will die." And it turns out to be a counter-intuitively very good sales pitch, and even more importantly, it's a good pitch for recruiting because... So it's a pitch to what? He had been at something called Y Combinator, which was a kind of factory for technology and technology investments—a development place, if you will. Incubator? Incubator, right. And he begins something called OpenAI, which is what, Ronan? Well, it started out as a nonprofit. And this is really at the heart of the story. This is a distinction that Elon Musk is now suing Sam Altman and OpenAI over. Well, who was involved in OpenAI in the beginning? He saw the industry moving towards that, and an interesting thing happened where he had been very optimistic about technology in general. But he, I think, saw an inflection point, according to people we talked to who were in conversations with him at the time and knew his thinking, where a lot of the leaders in the industry were becoming more apocalyptic about it. And one of those people was Elon Musk. And in fact, the very people, the scientific researchers who were most capable of building advanced AI, were some of the most freaked out about it. Why couldn't they do both from the start? "And it can't just be about racing to get the technology first; this has to be a scenario where we're willing to slow down on development to keep it safe." It was all rooted in a fear-driven argument. And that was why Google was the bad guy, because Google is for-profit—they're the mega-corporation—so we're going to be the good guys. Yeah. And that was, as Andrew alluded to, a really powerful recruiting tool. Because part of OpenAI's strength was not only did Sam Altman, through these quite extraordinary powers of persuasion, present this sort of fear-driven rationale why he needed to get the money for this, he also was able to go to the brightest minds in the field and say, "This is a nonprofit; we may not be able to pay as much as Google, but we can give you something else, which is—we're the good guys." So what happened a few years ago? The blip? Yeah, what was the blip? And you obviously did, with much greater time and access. And who is the guy you met, and how did your sense of him develop? Ronan, I've really thought about this very deeply. You know, this was not a story where we were following a single smoking gun piece of evidence where, you know, something dropped in our lap and we were direct. It—we actually did something that is very unusual in the current state of journalism, and we were able to take a year-plus just looking at AI and then seeing what questions emerged. And Sam Altman emerges as a question precisely because of what you described. People find it hard to wrap their arms around exactly what's going on with his motives. He is able to, and inclined to, tell different groups of people possibly conflicting things that make them all feel that they have the same concerns he has. That is an extraordinarily useful skill for a business person. But over time, what one person after another—I would say a majority of the sources we talked to—found is that they just couldn't rely on any baseline of truth. To an extraordinary extent, even on small things that are completely unnecessary to have any deception about—you know, we talk about an instance where he's in an office with colleagues apparently claiming that he was, like, a champion competitive ping pong player. Altman says that this was probably a joke, but people thought it was serious enough that they were struck by the fact that then he was one of the worst ping pong players in the office. Microsoft has been a huge funder of OpenAI with a lot of exclusive access to their own products. And geopolitically it's worth noting: a big thread in this piece is, in addition to talking about some of those early ideas that were raised about, you know, pitting powers against each other, there is still a present-day reality of this computational power becoming, as Sam has said, "the new currency of the world." It's the factor that may shape the balance of power between nations. And so there are people within this industry and within the national security establishment who feel that this unrestrained and, in Altman's case, often very nakedly transactional approach to getting as much money as possible—which inevitably in the current reality means doubling down on Middle Eastern money... There are those critics who say that is concentrating a new kind of power that is incredibly geopolitically sensitive under autocracies that may eventually be beyond our control. That you could wind up with a situation where a dictator has a disproportionate ownership of the most powerful technology on Earth and that that could pose a real national security threat. But that's where the money is. And that is where the money is. And we should say it's become standard in Silicon Valley to fundraise from the Middle East. Hollywood, everywhere. Everywhere. Also, where you get your funding for a movie is different than where you put, as some people call it, "a country of geniuses" in a data center. In other words, if you really think that you're growing a new form of superintelligence, you want to keep it in the family. Exactly. I'm going to ask you both this. First, Ronan, I get the sense that we all know what Elon Musk at this point means and what Elon Musk wants. Sam Altman is more mysterious, it seems to me. Ronan, what does Sam Altman want? What's the grand ambition here? To be the richest man in the world? At some point, he says that he was more interested in power than he is in money. What's your sense of him? I don't think it's just a caricature or hyperbole to say Sam Altman very often wants what you want in this moment. And you know, that is the crux of the matter. Sam Altman, more often than not, it seems across all of this documentation and sourcing, reads what the person on the other side of the table needs and wants, and he tells them those are the things he wants and believes in. Give me an example. Well, the entire founding story of the company—that at a time when safety fears had run wild and were kind of the animating engine of discourse, he was the safety guy and he told everyone that was his deepest belief. Now he says, and not totally unfairly, "Evolution over time is a real thing and I've had to adapt to changing circumstances." But even allowing for that—in both that big picture where he's gone from being the safety guy to being the—he would dispute this, but I would say fairly accurate to say—no regulation guy. And then they called in another executive who Altman had said was the source of this rumor, and that executive said, "I never said that," and Altman said, "Well, I never said that either," according to an account from someone there. And we talked about all the people involved; you can see the exact reporting, but the gist is, you know, he will often in the same moment reflect the different views and desires. He is a profoundly, by his own telling, conflict-averse person. And I think the piece holds a lot of sympathy for the reality of that emotionally and the understandability of that, the relatability of that, but it also doesn't explain it away. This is a trait that is present in him to a truly extraordinary extent that has ramifications for his businesses and for the world. Andrew? Yeah, I think it's important to grasp how much of the initial pitch for this was based on this view of the world that has completely flipped over time.
