Has Trump’s ‘Liberation Day’ tariffs helped US trade? - Asia Specific podcast, BBC World Service

BBC World Service(2.0M) ·22:02 ·14.0K views · 2026-04-10
Donald Trump (US President)
Following a US Supreme Court ruling that declared President Trump’s “Liberation Day” tariffs illegal, the administration has pivoted to a temporary 10% global tariff while attempting to navigate a massive $150 billion refund process; meanwhile, the p...
TL;DR Following a US Supreme Court ruling that declared President Trump’s “Liberation Day” tariffs illegal, the administration has pivoted to a temporary 10% global tariff while attempting to navigate a massive $150 billion refund process; meanwhile, the policy has successfully...

TL;DR

Following a US Supreme Court ruling that declared President Trump’s “Liberation Day” tariffs illegal, the administration has pivoted to a temporary 10% global tariff while attempting to navigate a massive $150 billion refund process; meanwhile, the policy has successfully rerouted global supply chains but failed to significantly restore domestic US manufacturing.

Current Legal and Regulatory Status

  • Supreme Court Ruling: The US Supreme Court struck down the use of the International Emergency Economic Powers Act (IEEPA), the primary mechanism for the original "Liberation Day" tariffs, declaring their application illegal.
  • The 10% "Holding Action": In response to the ruling, the US has transitioned to a temporary global tariff of 10% on most imports while developing a new, long-term set of rules.
  • The Refund Crisis: Deborah Elms notes that the US Treasury is currently mired in lawsuits and logistical hurdles regarding how to refund over $150 billion in illegally collected tariffs plus interest.
  • Sector-Specific Escalations: Despite the general 10% rate, certain sectors face much higher barriers:
      • Metals: Tariffs on solid metal products have reached approximately 50%.
      • Pharmaceuticals: Branded drugs face 100% tariffs unless companies fulfill specific US investment commitments.
      • Furniture & Cabinetry: These sectors remain subject to high, specific domestic protection tariffs.

Economic Impact and Trade Deficit Reality

  • Trade Deficit Shift: While the US trade deficit with China fell by 30% to its lowest level in two decades, this did not represent a total reduction in imports.
  • Supply Chain Rerouting: Demand has largely shifted from China to ASEAN nations.
  • ASEAN Growth: Increased demand for goods from Vietnam and Thailand has offset the drop in Chinese imports, as many Chinese firms moved production to these regions to bypass US tariffs.
  • Import Records: Despite "tariff headwinds," the US imported more goods last year than ever before, driven by a lack of domestic alternatives for consumer goods like apparel and electronics.
  • The "Globalization" Trade-off: Nick Marsh argues that bringing manufacturing back to the US would require American consumers to accept significantly higher prices and lower variety to offset the cost of US labor.

Regional Responses and the "Napkin" Deals

  • Fragile Agreements: Many Asian governments signed what Deborah Elms calls "napkin deals"—thin, non-binding, one-sided agreements intended to placate the Trump administration during the 90-day tariff pauses.
  • Investment "Sticks": The US is increasingly using tariffs as a "stick" to enforce investment promises. For example, pharmaceutical companies are given limited windows to break ground on US factories or face 100% tariff penalties.
  • Strategic Diversification: Recognizing US volatility, Asian nations are strengthening "bloc-to-bloc" relationships. Even historically protectionist nations like India are aggressively pursuing deals with the European Union and Southeast Asian partners.
  • Sector Resilience: The global AI and electronics boom has continued largely because semiconductors and chips have been maintained at or near 0% tariffs to avoid disrupting high-tech supply chains.

Notable Quotes

"We've basically seen eight decades of trade and economic integration that has been comprehensively smashed, and now you have to figure out how do we pick that back up and put it back together in a new way." — Deborah Elms, Hinrich Foundation
"Donald Trump is trying to turn the clock back on 20, 30, 40 years of globalization... He fundamentally is asking the American consumer to pay more for goods that they have been accustomed to paying very little for for a very, very long time." — Nick Marsh, BBC Business Reporter
"Trade just does find a way... China may be selling less to the United States, but it's less reliant on the United States. It's selling to Southeast Asia as well." — Nick Marsh, BBC Business Reporter
"The net result is, Trump says that he has reset the global economy, which I think is true... But where he has struggled is getting more jobs [and] more domestic manufacturing." — Deborah Elms, Hinrich Foundation
My fellow Americans, this is Liberation Day. Waiting for a long time. It's been just over a year since US President Donald Trump's Liberation Day, when he announced plans to impose tariffs on more than 90 countries. Our country and its taxpayers have been ripped off for more than 50 years, but it is not going to happen anymore. China was his key target, but other Asian countries also got hit hard. Starting tomorrow, the United States will implement reciprocal tariffs on other nations. The US tariffs threatened to upend global trade, but some predictions that the world economy would implode didn't end up happening. Then earlier this year, the US Supreme Court ruled the Liberation Day tariffs illegal. So where exactly are we now with tariffs? And with an energy crisis from the Iran War still very much affecting Asia, what's the outlook for the region? As always, I'm Mariko Oi in Singapore and this is Asia Specific from the BBC World Service. Twice a week we bring you Asia-Pacific stories unpacked by those who know them best. Today I have two guests who've been monitoring this tariff situation very, very closely. Deborah Elms is the head of trade policy at the Hinrich Foundation in Singapore, and Nick Marsh is a business reporter here who's been talking to businesses across the region about the impact of those tariffs. Welcome both. Thank you. Hello. So I remember covering this Liberation Day tariff. I think it was probably one of the busiest days of my career. But since then, Asian governments have been scrambling to cut deals with the United States while Mr. Trump chopped and changed, backtracked on some tariffs, while threatening to impose even more. So Deborah, where exactly are we at with US tariffs on Asian countries? Most of those Liberation Day tariffs that you just talked about a year ago were imposed under one domestic US law, and that law was struck down by the US Supreme Court. So the top court in the land said that that law, the use of tariffs under that law, was illegal. That meant that the administration had to stop collecting a variety of tariffs against Asian countries and instead switch to something else. And so they stopped using what was called IEEPA, the International Emergency Economic Powers Act, and they moved to some other temporary holding action basically at 10% globally. So all trade into the US, with some exceptions, is at 10% tariffs. Some of those exceptions are in specific sectors, so we have sector-specific tariffs on things like metals and furniture and kitchen cabinets. But also we have country-specific tariffs that are currently sitting at 10%. We're waiting for a transition in the next couple of months to some longer-term set of tariff rules that will allow the president to go back to what he thinks are fair tariffs. Those were imposed on Asia ranging from 40 to 10%. So Nick, let's remind our viewers and listeners, because we haven't actually talked about tariffs much. What exactly did Donald Trump want to achieve by imposing these tariffs? Yeah, I mean it's funny, when I tell people I was doing this podcast, Mariko, a lot, the first thing a lot of people said was, "Tariffs? That's a word I haven't heard for a while." I'm sure that's not the same for you, Deborah. No, every day. But as you know, general business and economics reporters, you know, the news cycle moves on. We've been talking a lot about the war in the Middle East recently and the price of oil and things like that. But tariffs arguably are one of the most seismic policy shifts that we've seen in the global economy. To put it very simply, Donald Trump took a look at the state of US manufacturing and he saw that the US wasn't making anywhere near as much as it was before. And crucially, it was importing way more than it was before, particularly from China and from Asia generally. And that was causing a trade deficit, so importing more than you're selling. He said that was bad for the American economy. He said that was bad for American workers, that they were losing their jobs essentially to Asian workers. The way that he thought, and his advisors thought, they would rectify that was to make it really, really expensive to buy foreign goods. And that's a tariff. It's what the importer pays in order to be able to get that good into the United States. The long-term effect of that, he argues, is that businesses will then be encouraged to set up more factories, employ more American workers, and basically make things in the USA as opposed to getting them from outside. Whether he has achieved those aims and whether as a general economic ideology that is sound anyway in the 21st century is a whole other discussion. A lot of people disagree with that, don't they, Deborah? They do. And there's one other additional piece, which was revenue. Because those tariffs that are paid by American businesses and American consumers on imports go straight to the US Treasury Department. It was a way for them to collect a lot of money in a pretty short amount of time because tariffs went from an average of 2% before Liberation Day all the way up, well at one point we were at 145% tariffs on Chinese goods, and on average closer to 20%. And so you can imagine that if you go from 2% to 20%, the amount of revenue that has been collected by the US has been substantial. Most of that is now under dispute and needs to be refunded. The complexities of refunding, you know, 150 plus billion dollars worth of tariffs is, as you can imagine, occupying a huge amount of time and effort right now. How do you do that? How do you do that with interest? Who gets it, who doesn't get it? It's the subject of lots of lawsuits. But in terms of the trade deficit that Nick talked about, I mean that was kind of the pet hate of President Trump, wasn't it? And last year US trade deficit against China fell by 30% and the deficit was the smallest in around two decades. And also I remember some Asian governments like Cambodia cutting tariffs on US imports to zero as part of their negotiation. So can you argue that maybe he got what he wanted to achieve to an extent? No, because the numbers show that the US actually imported more goods, trading goods. We're not talking about services or anything else, just trading goods. The US imported more trade in goods last year than ever before. In spite of all of these tariff headwinds and all of the additional costs, US consumers and US businesses still bought from overseas suppliers. They did that for lots of reasons. One is that there aren't domestic producers of a lot of the things that Americans buy or want to buy. And if there are domestic producers, they tend to be much more expensive. You can imagine like having to pay an American worker versus paying say a Cambodian worker to make a t-shirt. We're looking at wildly different costs of that t-shirt. And so the net result is, Trump says that he has reset the global economy, which I think is true actually, that part he succeeded. But where he has struggled is the getting more jobs, more domestic manufacturing. So what would it take for him to achieve that, to bring jobs back to the US if not tariffs? I suppose you could use tariffs to get there, but they would have to be at a significantly higher rate, right? It would have to be so high that the producer of, you know, sofas and t-shirts and microphones would say we can no longer afford at 100% tariffs to import these. We will have to open up factories and Americans will have to, as Trump himself said, be satisfied with a lot less. $2 instead of I think he said $37 or something, right? Something like that. So you would have to, the trade-off is you would have to accept higher prices and less of lots of different kinds of things in exchange for having these jobs and this manufacturing take place in the United States. And this has been the big sticking point really because, you know, in so many words, Donald Trump is trying to turn the clock back on 20, 30, 40 years of globalization and of outsourcing of jobs to less developed countries, you know, and that includes China as well. And he fundamentally is asking the American consumer to pay more for goods that they have been accustomed to paying very little for for a very, very long time. Because somewhere the shortfall has to be made up, you know, if the wages are higher, or he's asking the American worker to be paid less. That's a simple, that's a you know slightly reductive way of looking at it, but that is essentially where the tariff argument kind of ends up. Yeah, and that was the big warning, wasn't it? That this would feed into the cost of living crisis that American consumers were already feeling. I want to just go back on this US trade deficit against China though. How did they manage to fall by 30%? What exactly happened there, Deb? Well we'll be arguing about this for a long time to come. I'm sure there's PhD dissertations waiting to be written. But there's at least three explanations for what happened. One is that either Chinese firms stopped marketing to the US because, especially once tariffs hit 145%, again it was temporary but it was a headline figure. They stopped marketing and or American firms simply stopped buying from China. So there is a certain amount of just reduction in demand both ways. The second is that a lot of things that used to be bought from China directly were now shipped to the United States and kept in inventory in the US. So a lot of things, especially like packages out of Shein and Temu for lower-priced clothing, used to be ordered directly from China and then airfreighted to the US. Now they were sent by ship, offloaded into the US and then distributed from the US. And so the figures again suggest we had a fair amount of that taking place in some sectors. And then the third thing is, because there was an incentive at 145% for China and 20% say for Vietnam was the tariff on Vietnam, there was a lot of incentive for firms to say let me use Vietnamese manufacturing as a location and then send to the US. Some of that is local Vietnamese investment. Some of that, those are Chinese companies that have moved into Vietnam to make the thing that they then send to the US. So if you look at the numbers, the fall in demand US-China is largely offset by the rise in demand from ASEAN into the US. And so on balance you've shifted the demand around, you've shifted those supply chains, but you haven't fundamentally reduced the demand in the US for these kinds of products that are supplied out of Asia. I remember there were so many warnings that these tariffs could completely, you know, upend the global economy, could result in job losses and so on and so on. That didn't exactly happen though, did it? And I was reading how global trade grew by almost 5% in 2025. So Nick, why is that? Yeah, I mean we were just talking about it very briefly before we started recording. Like trade just does find a way, you know. People need goods, people need to pay a certain amount for goods. So I'd say there were two things that have happened. The first thing is that yes, what was previously made in China might now be made in Vietnam or Thailand or you know depending what you're talking about, and then going to the United States. The second thing is that producers find new markets. You know, there used to be a time when the United States was the most lucrative consumer market, the richest market, the most voracious market. So yes we have a number of deals ranging from what I called long ago napkin deals, which are literally like as durable as a napkin, to those that are a little more robust. But we're still we're not talking about normal traditional trade agreements that run to hundreds of pages and are legally binding. These are all very small, very thin. And a number of them have been signed. They are very one-sided documents. And once IEEPA fell, this statute that gave the president the authority to impose these tariffs, a lot of the governments that signed them said, "Well what happens to the document that we signed based on our understanding that this was all under IEEPA?" The US continues to maintain that the deals are the deals. None of these deals, just to be clear, are actually in force, none of them. Has money been spent or are countries waiting to see what happens with what happens with the Supreme Court ruling for example? I think we've had a mixed assessment. A lot of existing investments got repackaged and put into this. Those may be continuing. The Trump administration recognizes the danger of backsliding on these investment commitments. So one area where they have just last week really tried to clamp down on this is in pharmaceuticals. So the United States said we're going to impose a 100% tariffs on branded pharmaceuticals into the United States unless your firm has made commitments to invest. And if you've made an investment commitment, then your tariffs are down 20%, could be 0%. But if you've only just promised it and you haven't delivered it, there's like a stick at the end. You have this much time in which to make that investment start happening, otherwise you go back up to 100%. And I think it's part of the Trump administration's plan to solve backsliding on these investment commitments because they are I think understandably nervous that partners are going to say, "Why am I investing, especially why why am I investing potentially our taxpayer dollars into the US economy at a time of disruption working with this particular trade partner? Like why?" And we came to the table based on threats like you said that didn't actually materialize in the end, or which are uncertain at the moment. But it's worth reiterating, you know, these are commitments, investment commitments, but these are jobs. You know, these are jobs, these are company's fortunes, these are like you said taxpayer dollars as well. You know, these are these are big amounts of money which are just kind of in the air. I mean Donald Trump really seems to love tariffs. I mean it seems to be one of his most favorite words. Is there method to this strategy? If you had to pick one, what would it be? Well I think if there was a clear strategy that worked, we would see it replicated elsewhere, right? We would see other governments who say hey this tariff thing is not such a bad idea, we will impose them. And we have not seen that. Other governments have said no, we may not allow free flow of goods, and no one has ever allowed fully free flow of goods, but we're not going to use tariffs as the way to make that change. And so I think Trump is in has been embarking on an experiment that so far doesn't appear to have achieved its own objectives and hasn't really been a policy that other governments think, "Let me replicate that." In fact, I would say the opposite. One point I would make a year into this process is that Asia's response is to yes, deal with Donald Trump, placate him if you must, do whatever it is, but in the meantime beef up your relations with others. And so we see a tremendous amount happening in the last year on a bilateral basis. I mean even India, which has been incredibly hostile to trade for a very long time, has gone on a stampede to sign with the European Union and lots of other trading partners. The Southeast Asian countries are becoming more connected as well with one another. They're looking at these regional blocs. We're talking about bloc-to-bloc discussions now. So I think there has been a lot of activity in the trade and economic space without the US, and sometimes without China, although that's a bit of a more blurry picture. But you can clearly see the reconfiguration starting. And I think, I mean we're at the early stages of this. We've basically seen eight decades of trade and economic integration that has been comprehensively smashed, and now you have to figure out how do we pick that back up and put it back together in a new way. It will not look like it was. But what will that look like? Yeah, new relationships aren't they being being formed all the time, right? Out of necessity. One thing I would add to that is, you know, tariffs are not new, of course. Other countries impose tariffs, right? We have tariffs. We saw the European Union put pretty high tariffs on Chinese electric vehicles. Governments want to protect their own industries, whether that's through subsidies, they want to protect their workers. That that is pretty standard, you know, government policy. It's the aggressiveness with which Donald Trump imposed these tariffs that shocked a lot of people and the radical results he wanted to achieve through this strategy which has upended a lot of these relationships. TL;DR

Q1 2026 was defined by geopolitical volatility in Iran, a significant correction in software stocks driven by AI-related pricing fears, and a liquidity crunch in private credit marked by record-high redemption requests reaching as high as 41%.

Geopolitical Impact & Macroeconomic Outlook

  • Iran Ceasefire: A two-week "fragile truce" and pledge to reopen the Strait of Hormuz led to a 15% collapse in oil prices and a 2.5% single-day S&P bounce. However, tensions remain high as Iran claims US violations and disputes whether the ceasefire applies to Lebanon.
  • Market Indices: Despite a strong January, the S&P 500 closed Q1 down 4% and the Nasdaq closed down 7%.
  • Interest Rate Threshold: The 10-year Treasury yield is currently the primary market driver.
      • The 4.5% Boundary: Eisman identifies 4.5% as a critical psychological and performance barrier; once breached, equity markets tend to sell off aggressively.
  • Energy Sector Dominance: Energy was the top-performing sector (+37%), driven entirely by war premiums. Eisman notes that while US Energy is the size of the entire Saudi Arabian economy, it only represents 4% of the S&P 500.

The Private Capital Crisis: Redemptions & "Volatility Laundering"

  • Private Credit Liquidity Strain: Major funds are facing unprecedented redemption requests far exceeding their caps.
      • Blue Owl: The flagship fund (OCIC) saw 21.9% redemption requests, while its tech fund (OTIC) saw a staggering 41%. Both capped redemptions at 5%.
      • Carlyle: The CTAC fund received 16% redemption notices, also honoring only 5%.
      • Rating Agency Shifts: Moody’s moved the outlook for Blue Owl and the entire BDC (Business Development Company) industry to negative.
  • Private Equity’s "Intellectual Disguise": Private Equity grew from $4 trillion in 2016 to $9 trillion in 2025.
      • Volatility Laundering: Eisman argues PE growth is driven by the "opacity" of valuations. Because they don't mark-to-market daily, they appear to have higher Sharpe ratios (better risk-adjusted returns) than public markets, which he labels a "disguise."
  • Underwater Tech Deals: PE firms went on a "buying binge" of software companies between 2018–2022. With the AI-driven shift in software valuations, Eisman asserts every one of those transactions is likely "underwater by a lot."

AI Disruption & The Software "Humbling"

  • Software Multiple Collapse: For the first time in decades, software price-to-earnings (P/E) multiples are lower than the market multiple.
  • The SaaS Thesis at Risk: The subscription model (SaaS) relied on two variables: growing "seat" counts and annual price increases.
      • AI Threat: AI-generated software threatens to lower the cost of entry and eliminate the ability for incumbents to raise prices, striking at the heart of the software investment thesis.
  • White-Collar AI Layoffs: Eisman highlights a growing list of companies announcing mass layoffs attributed to AI or restructuring:
      • UPS: 48,000 workers.
      • Intel: 24,000 workers.
      • Citigroup: 20,000 workers.
      • Block: 4,000 workers (nearly half its workforce).

Q1 Sector Performance Summary

  • Winners: Energy (+37%), Materials (+9%), Utilities (+8%), and Consumer Staples (+7%). Gains in Utilities and Staples reflect a "flight to safety."
  • Losers: Financials (-10%), Info Tech (-9%), and Consumer Discretionary (-9%).
  • Healthcare Divergence: Traditionally a safety play, Healthcare fell 5%. Health insurers like Humana (-32%) and UnitedHealth (-18%) struggled with rising costs and Medicare Advantage pricing pressure, though a recent government pricing update (2.5% vs. 0.09% original estimate) provided some late-quarter relief.

I don't speak about private equity as frequently as private credit because private credit seems to be where all the risk is buried. Since private equity companies own many private credit funds, it is worth taking a detour today to look more closely at the huge growth in private equity. Since the great financial crisis, private equity has been one of the fastest-growing asset classes. According to McKinsey, private equity was 4 trillion in 2016 and reached almost 9 trillion in 2025. Why has private equity grown so much? It's partially because of an intellectual disguise. Institutional investors care greatly about their Sharpe ratio. What's that? The Sharpe ratio is a financial metric that is used to evaluate an investment return relative to its risk. It calculates the excess return per unit of volatility. A high Sharpe ratio would be the result, for example, of strong returns coupled with low volatility. Every institutional investor covets that. Now for many institutional investors, post-great financial crisis, a great appeal of private equity is, ironically, its opacity. Investing in public markets provides a valuation every day. Investing in private equity appears to create smoother volatility because prices are given only on occasion. This allowed institutional investors to claim that they had greater Sharpe ratios than they did when they invested more in public markets pre-GFC. Some commentators call this volatility laundering. Now it's totally true that the private equity strategy worked very well for many years by employing leverage during periods of low rates and financial repression. That private equity is also less volatile is just plain wrong. Just because you don't get a daily price does not mean that the asset class is less volatile. It just appears to be less volatile. And now the private equity sector is buried in software. So much for the Sharpe ratio. Let's get back to private credit. Not a week goes by without new bad news from the world of private credit. At the end of last week, Blue Owl announced that it had received elevated redemption requests for two of its private credit funds for the first quarter of 2026. The firm's flagship, OCIC, with about 36 billion in assets under management, received redemption requests of an astonishing 21.9%. Blue Owl's smaller tech-oriented fund, OTIC, which is 3.3 billion in size, received redemption requests of 41% during the same period. The firm capped redemptions at 5%. Wow. These redemption levels are unheard of. Also this week, Carlyle's flagship private credit fund, the Carlyle Tactical Private Credit Fund, CTAC, with 7 billion in assets, got a 16% redemption notice, but only honored the 5% cap. Also this week, Moody's moved the outlook on the Blue Owl OCIC fund to negative because of the high redemption requests. Moody's also changed its outlook for the entire BDC industry to negative, also because of elevated redemptions. This is typical of the rating agencies who are notoriously slow to change their ratings. However, and this is very important, there was finally some good news in the world of private credit. Because of all the bad publicity, credit spreads have been widening, thereby making future private credit loans potentially more lucrative. Credit spreads widening is an indication of fear and stress in debt markets. When credit spreads get sufficiently wide, they can become attractive to investors. I will speak about this concept in much greater detail in a future masterclass lecture about debt and credit. On Tuesday, Blackstone announced that it closed a 10 billion dollar opportunistic credit fund. The company said the fund, called Blackstone Capital Opportunities Fund V, was oversubscribed. This fund was sold only to institutional investors. In addition, Goldman announced that it had already lined up more than 10 billion in institutional commitments for a new direct lending drawdown fund in just a few weeks. Now let's talk about the first quarter. The first quarter is over, and earnings season starts next week. So it's a good time to take a step back and review what has happened, which sectors and companies have done well and not so well, and what lessons can we learn. This was a tough quarter, but it did not start out that way. In January, the S&P was up a bit over 1%. Thereafter, the market began to wobble because of fears about the impact of AI on multiple sectors and also the potential for rising losses in private credit. There seemed to be daily stories on both of these issues. For example, Anthropic seemed to announce a new product almost daily, products that might impact insurance brokers, wealth managers, software, etc. Investors freaked out on every story. Bad news on private credit was an almost daily event. But those two risks were shunted off of the front page by the war in Iran. Before the war began, the 10-year was at 3.95% and oil prices were around 70 dollars. Oil prices jumped above 100, and that created fear that inflation was just around the corner. Now normally in times of stress, investors buy Treasuries, but not this time. The 10-year got as high as 4.4%. The combination of higher oil prices and increasing interest rates was just too much. Over the last four years, 4.5% on the 10-year has proven to be an important boundary for equity performance. Once the 10-year breaches 4.5%, the market tends to sell off. We got close. Yes, energy prices are important, but the US economy is so large and diversified that a sector the size of Saudi Arabia is just a small part of our overall economy. Decades ago, software companies sold software on a one-time sales basis. Gradually, the industry moved to the SaaS model, software as a subscription service. That model is easier for sell-side and buy-side analysts to model. It also makes it easier to slowly but continuously raise prices. The combination created a multi-decade bull market in software. Not only did the stocks go up, but the multiples expanded as well. That's why the advent of AI strikes at the heart of the entire software investment thesis. It hurts it on multiple levels. The biggest fear is that AI-generated software is much cheaper and will just replace existing software completely. Now I think that is somewhat unlikely. There is more to being a successful software company than just creating software. What I think is most realistic is this: when you model a software company, the two biggest variables are the growth in seats and the average price paid per seat. Multiply the estimate of the total seats by the average price per seat and you get a revenue estimate. It's quite elegant. Prior to AI, the assumption for software companies was that seats would increase and pricing would go up every year. Both of those assumptions are now at risk. Seat growth could slow, but what is most likely is that the ability to raise prices weakens enormously. Because of all these fears, software stocks have been obliterated and that is why the entire Info Tech group was down 9% in the first quarter. Inside Arm’s AI Pivot: From Smartphones to the Cloud | Bloomberg Tech: Europe 4/10/2026

Bloomberg Technology(720.0K) ·24:04 ·1.6K views · 2026-04-10
Tom Mackenzie (Host, Bloomberg) Rene Haas (CEO, Arm)
Arm is executing a massive strategic pivot from licensing smartphone chip designs to becoming a primary producer of high-end AI silicon, targeting a data center market opportunity projected to exceed $100 billion.
TL;DR Arm is executing a massive strategic pivot from licensing smartphone chip designs to becoming a primary producer of high-end AI silicon, targeting a data center market opportunity projected to exceed $100 billion. By launching its own "AGI CPU" and selling directly to...

TL;DR

Arm is executing a massive strategic pivot from licensing smartphone chip designs to becoming a primary producer of high-end AI silicon, targeting a data center market opportunity projected to exceed $100 billion. By launching its own "AGI CPU" and selling directly to hyperscalers like Meta and OpenAI, Arm is transitioning from a neutral "Switzerland" of the industry to a direct competitor with traditional giants like Intel and AMD.

Strategic Shift: From Licensing to Silicon Production

  • The AGI CPU Launch: For the first time in its 35-year history, Arm is moving beyond intellectual property (IP) licensing to sell its own in-house processor, the AGI CPU.
      • Manufacturing Partner: The chips are designed by Arm and manufactured by TSMC.
      • Target Market: Specifically designed for artificial intelligence data centers, with early customers including Meta, SAP, Cloudflare, and OpenAI.
  • Ending "Neutrality": Industry analysts note this move ends Arm’s status as the "Switzerland" of semiconductors.
      • Competitive Risk: By selling finished hardware, Arm now competes with long-term partners like Intel and AMD, as well as some of its own licensees who may feel pressured to build their own silicon.
      • Defensive Necessity: Investors like Claire Pleydell-Bouverie (Liontrust) argue this move was necessary to prevent "royalty erosion," where customers were using bare architecture licenses to pay Arm less.
  • Agentic AI Demands: CEO Rene Haas noted that "agentic AI" (AI that performs autonomous tasks) quadruples the demands placed on CPUs, creating an urgent market need for more efficient, high-performance hardware.

Market Potential and Financial Outlook

  • TAM Expansion: Arm projects the shift will move them from a $3 billion Total Addressable Market (TAM) in traditional cloud royalties to a hardware-based TAM exceeding $100 billion within five years.
  • SoftBank Relationship: Despite being publicly listed, Arm remains 90% owned by SoftBank.
      • Vision vs. Earnings: Haas credits Masayoshi Son’s long-term "90% ownership" for allowing the company to ignore quarterly pressures and focus on multi-year technology cycles.
  • Global Scale: To date, 350 billion Arm-based chips have been produced—triple the total number of humans who have ever lived.

Geopolitics and Regional Competition

  • Political Oversight: Haas highlights a significant shift in the CEO role; five years ago, government relations were a minor concern, but today, navigating Washington D.C. and London’s "Number 10" is a top priority as chips become tools of statecraft.
  • Europe’s Decline: Haas concurs with assessments that Europe has fallen behind the US and China in semiconductor innovation.
      • Infrastructure Gaps: The issue is attributed not to a lack of talent, but to poorly organized capital and a lack of infrastructure to nurture startups into scale-ups.
  • Stargate and Infrastructure: Addressing rumors of "Stargate" (the massive OpenAI/Oracle infrastructure project) faltering, Haas dismissed the concerns as "overblown," noting that capital for compute remains the primary bottleneck for AI growth.

The Future of Work and AI Scaling

  • Workforce Impact: Haas is an AI optimist, arguing that while AI may automate tasks, it will not reduce Arm’s headcount.
      • Innovation Bottleneck: Arm’s current limitation is a shortage of engineers to execute on ideas; AI is being used internally to design better products faster, not to reduce operating expenses (OpEx).
  • AI vs. Previous Cycles: Haas describes the AI boom as fundamentally different from the PC or mobile internet cycles due to its sheer scale and the fact that it touches "every facet of human life."

Notable Quotes

"We are shifting away from being largely known for smartphones to being around the cloud and AI data centers... in five years it'll be orders of magnitude larger than what we do in smartphones." — Rene Haas, CEO, Arm
"Arm has got the software ecosystem—22 million developers... it’s got the IP and now they’ve got the hardware. They’ve got the CPU now... it places Arm in a pole position for agentic AI." — Claire Pleydell-Bouverie, Fund Manager, Liontrust
"Up until now Arm has been this kind of provider of technology in the background... now they’re kind of stepping out into the light and they’re saying 'Hey, here we are; we’re a real company.'" — Ian King, Semiconductor Analyst, Bloomberg
"There's 117 billion humans that ever lived on Earth ever, and we're [350 billion chips] three times that number. That scale is enormous." — Rene Haas, CEO, Arm
You are watching Bloomberg Tech Europe. Coming up, it powers the vast majority of smartphones worldwide and a growing share of AI data centers. Arm, the company underpinning the chips used by Apple, Nvidia, and Microsoft, is now making one of the boldest moves in its history. Arm is going from purely designing chips to making them in a bid to command AI like it's dominated smartphones. It is a high-stakes shift, but the company's chief executive says it could drive strong growth for years to come. We are shifting away from being largely known for smartphones to being around the cloud and AI data centers where in a few years that's our largest business by far, and in five years it'll be orders of magnitude larger than what we do in smartphones. So will Arm's big AI gamble pay off? We put that question to its CEO Rene Haas. We'll also hear from investors and industry experts on the future of this unique company. Welcome to Bloomberg Tech Europe. This is Cambridge, England, home to Arm, one of the world's most influential chip companies. Arm was founded in 1990 with a radically different vision from Silicon Valley's chip giants. Instead of making processors, it would design them, focusing on efficiency, not just raw power. That low-power approach combined with a licensing model meant Arm's designs could be used in almost any device and end up anywhere. Arm's model proved ideal for mobile phones, and when smartphones took off, Arm's designs were already built-in. Today nearly every smartphone in the world, from Apple to Xiaomi, runs on Arm-based technology. Arm went public in 1998 in both London and New York at the height of the dotcom boom. Eighteen years later, it was taken private for 32 billion dollars by Japan's SoftBank. Former Nvidia executive Rene Haas took over as chief executive as CEO in 2022 and returned Arm to the public markets just a year later. This time, Arm listed in New York only, a major blow to London. Now Arm is making one of the biggest strategic bets in the company's history. The company recently unveiling its first ever in-house processor, the AGI CPU, designed for artificial intelligence data centers, marking a move from licensing into selling silicon itself. From smartphones to the infrastructure powering AI, then, Arm's stronghold on global computing continues to grow. Now Arm may be listed in the US, but it is very much a company with global reach. The company sits at the center of Masayoshi Son's ambitions to take on the AI hyperscalers. Its parent SoftBank still owns roughly 90 percent even after Arm's 2023 IPO. I traveled to San Francisco to speak with CEO Rene Haas about the company's pivotal shift. "Well, what's happening inside Arm is we are shifting away from being largely known for smartphones to being around the cloud and AI data centers. In a few years that's our largest business by far, and in five years it'll be orders of magnitude larger than what we do in smartphones." For the first time, Arm is designing its own chips, its own CPUs, the whole package, and you're working with Meta for this. How significant is that for this company as a strategy? "I think it's very significant when we looked at the opportunity particularly in the data center with the growth of AI but more specifically agentic AI, and agentic AI quadruples the demands on CPUs." "We looked at two things. Number one, this is a market that's heavily underserved at the moment, and number two, customers are asking us, 'Would you deliver and build a chip for us?' So to your point, yes, we now have our first product. It's Arm's product. It's the Arm AGI CPU designed by Arm, built by TSMC, and sold to customers like Meta or SAP or Cloudflare." "OpenAI is a litany of customers that we've announced, and the demand has been extremely strong." What is the potential size of this new revenue stream, of this new market for you? "When we look at the royalties in our traditional business model associated with the cloud and AI business, it's about a 3 billion dollar TAM. When we look at the potential of this product line, maybe stretching out four or five years, it's north of 100 billion dollars. So it is profoundly different in terms of the scale. It puts us in a whole different zip code." Semiconductors have increasingly become a tool of geopolitics as well. How challenging does that make your role as CEO of a company that has that global scope? "Five years ago, maybe maybe a little bit less, it wasn't something we worried about. We may have had people inside Washington or working with people at Number 10 to make them aware of the technology, but it wasn't something that was top on the radar." "Visiting Washington or getting to know the folks inside DCMS inside the UK, that was not something that as a CEO you'd have high on your priority list of things to do. That's all changed." Do you think governments understand where the real leverage is in the chip stack? "I think they're increasingly learning that. I would say 10 years ago, not so much, but they've certainly added lots of capability in terms of people." "If you look at the US government in particular and the current administration, they've added David Sacks who is the AI czar, and under David's team, he's put a tremendous amount of capability and people to go off and learn that. Same thing under Peter Kyle and then Liz Kendall in the UK." And you're stewarding this technology that underpins billions of devices. Do you personally, Rene Haas, feel the weight of that responsibility at moments when you think, "Wow"? "When you're in it on a day-to-day basis, you don't really think about that kind of scale. But sometimes when I step back and when we're talking amongst the team, we're preparing some materials for a launch and we try to communicate the scale of what Arm does." "We come up with a number of 350 billion chips. People like to talk about well, how many chips does a certain person use? 350 billion chips. There's 117 billion humans that ever lived on Earth ever, and we're three times that number. That scale is enormous." One of the fascinating parts of the Arm story is the nature of the business. Headquartered of course in Cambridge, United Kingdom, majority-owned by SoftBank, and publicly listed here in the US. How do you navigate having to meet quarterly earnings with Masayoshi Son's grand, sweeping visions of how this kind of tech plays out? "Yeah, I have a fortunate job in some senses in that SoftBank and Masa is a 90 percent owner of Arm. So I have investor meetings quite frequently with my chief shareholder. But he's very long on Arm, hasn't sold a share since we went public, and is very, very long on the potential. So it helps. It helps make my job in some ways easier, Tom, in the sense that he has a long-range vision as do I." "Technology we always know is a long, long game. It's not a real quarterly game, so it allows me to have some freedom to think about the long term without having a wide cadre of investors who worry about what's going on inside the quarter. Thankfully the quarters have been very good and we've met and or raised guidance nearly every quarter since we've gone public." What's going on with Stargate? OpenAI, Oracle... they seem to be pulling back on some of their commitments around Stargate, which is this massive infrastructure commitment for the US but also outside the US as well. Is that starting to falter? Are you concerned about what's going on with Stargate? "No, not at all. I mean, we, SoftBank, just put a large investment into OpenAI in the last round. They're going to be using a lot of that capital for more compute. OpenAI is a magnificent company and I think their growth potential is literally unbounded. Compute is what will limit them, so they're needing more and more compute. There's been a lot written about Stargate, but Stargate is a complex program. I think the noise made around the Stargate thing stumbling has been a bit overblown." Apple is a big customer, a big client clearly of Arm. Has Apple lost the AI race, do you think, Rene, or are they going to be a fast follower? What's your take? "Too early to tell. We are still in very early days of this AI race." Where do you stand on the question of AI and jobs? And a company like Arm, are you going to be in five to ten years' time... are you going to have more employees or fewer? "The way I think about AI eliminating jobs is when you think about again, let's use agentic AI as an example. An agent can go off and run a job or run a piece of work for you, and one might argue that well, that's going to eliminate jobs because all that work is now being done by an agent." "But as long as there are people coming up with creative ideas and are imaginative about how to grow—what other what new product could I design—things could just happen a lot faster. Inside Arm we are pushing it heavily. I'm pushing it very, very heavily, not to reduce Opex; it is to design products faster and to design better products quicker and innovate and have more products." "Right now, my limitation for developing great products is the amount of engineers that we have. That is my biggest limitation. And right now AI hasn't solved that. And it's again, it's not to replace the engineers because we've got no shortage of ideas. I've got tons and tons of ideas that we talk about in our leadership team meetings where we look and think, 'Gosh, we'd love to do X if only.'" "So I'm much more an optimist on this and I think so long as companies can come up with creative ideas on what to do and build, AI is going to suit us quite just well." I spoke to ASML's CEO Christophe Fouquet a few months ago and he said he was pretty damning about Europe's place in this semiconductor industry. He says Europe has no real capabilities to compete with the US and with China. Do you agree? Where can Europe compete when it comes to semiconductors? "I think Europe has fallen behind in this space. I think when you look at the amount of innovation that takes place inside either the US or China, Europe has fallen behind. I think some of it isn't actually at the big company level. I think some of it is at the startup company level. In other words, companies that can get funding early, do innovation, attract really smart people to go off and build companies and to be entrepreneurial." "Whether the reasons are there's not enough capital or that capital is not organized well enough, or there's something that is making founders move elsewhere. So I don't think it's a lack of talent. I think it's a lack of infrastructure to nurture the companies to start in Europe and stay in Europe." When it comes to the data center story, when do you think you'll be able to declare victory? What for you is success? You've again shifted Arm to have a laser-like focus on data centers to build out that market. When can you call that a win, do you think? "Well on one level I think it's a win now in that we've got real customers: Meta, SAP, Cloudflare, Cisco, as I mentioned. So on level zero it's a win. Going forward, I think there's lots of opportunity to do much, much, much more. The aperture for the opportunity for Arm has just got much, much, much wider. I mentioned north of 100 billion dollars for this AGI chip AGI CPU product, but if we think about the broader market it could be north of a trillion dollars that we could address." Arm CEO Rene Haas speaking to me in San Francisco. Arm's move to sell its own chips has been largely cheered by investors. Its stock surged after the announcement on March the 24th. One of those investors supportive of the plan is Claire Pleydell-Bouverie, fund manager at Liontrust. Liontrust's top tech investments include Nvidia, Broadcom, and TSMC. Pleydell-Bouverie says Arm's switch to produce its own AI CPUs is a significant moment in the company's history. Arm is now a full-stack provider of the CPU, and what we see time and time again with technology platform transitions is that the lion's share of the economics go to a handful of players. "These are full-stack players. Think of Microsoft in the PC era, Apple for the mobile, AWS for the cloud, and Arm has got the software ecosystem. You know, 22 million developers, that's over 80 percent of the global total. It's got the IP and now they've got the hardware. They've got the CPU now. The key question is what's more important? Designing the CPU first and establishing leadership—think of Intel, AMD—or building out the ecosystem first and then designing the chip?" "We actually think it's the latter because designing the chip, okay, it's hard, but it's not the hard part. Building an ecosystem takes years; it actually takes decades. If you think about CUDA, Nvidia's software ecosystem, that took two decades to build out. It's got over 5 million developers working within CUDA today. Arm has done the same over 35 years and that ecosystem is actually four times larger." "So we think this is very, very important for the ecosystem. It places Arm in a pole position in our view for agentic AI and AI at the edge." Is the cost to this for Arm that it can no longer claim to be the Switzerland of the semiconductor industry? Is that a risk for Arm? "This was the key risk and we think that Arm made the right call because they were essentially trapped. Arm's key customers were downgrading their relationship with Arm in order to pay them less, moving from pre-designed Arm CPU cores to designing their own CPU cores and just paying Arm the bare architecture license fee. Qualcomm was the canary in the coal mine there with their acquisition of Nuvia." "So Arm, if they weren't to make this move and launch the AGI CPU, we think they could have seen just a gentle erosion in royalty rates." Claire Pleydell-Bouverie, fund manager at Liontrust, speaking to me about Arm's prospects. Coming up, Arm CEO Rene Haas weighing in on the AI boom. "It feels different in the sense that it's just much bigger. So if you think about these cycles, whether it was the PCs going to mobile internet, now going to AI, again pattern matching is similar, but the scale is unlike anything we've seen before." We're bringing more from our interview with Rene Haas as he discusses the outlook for AI and his legacy. That is next. This is Bloomberg. As Arm moves into building its own chips, it estimates it could earn up to 15 billion dollars in revenue over the next five years. So what does that mean for rivals like Intel and AMD and for Arm's own growth ambitions? I spoke to Bloomberg's semiconductor czar Ian King to break it down. "I mean this is a seminal moment. This is something that's kind of been telegraphed for a while, but it really is... they're stepping over the line and they're really becoming a chipmaker." What does it mean for Arm, and what does it mean for the semiconductor industry more broadly? "Well, up until now Arm has been this kind of provider of technology in the background. Everybody uses them, they're everybody's friend, but now they're kind of stepping out into the light and they're saying, 'Hey, here we are, we're a real company.' So some of those previous customers are going to become competition, and they're making this bid, they're making this play for a share of AI." How do you think the competitors then, and let's start with AMD and Intel, how this impacts them? Do Arm take real market share from AMD and Intel? How do you expect the competitors on that front to respond to this? "I mean you could say that Arm already has taken share from those two guys because at one point the server processor market was 100 percent x86, which is 100 percent Intel's technology." "Because of AWS, because of other companies that are actually using Arm's technology, that market share dominance has already been eroded indirectly." Arm becomes a chipmaker. Hyperscalers are also trying on some levels to become chipmakers themselves. Does this decision by Arm to become a chipmaker rather than just providing the components accelerate the shift by hyperscalers to build out their own silicon, or does it put those plans on the back burner? "We'll see. I mean and this will have a heavy, heavy say in Arm's future, whether this is the right decision or whether it becomes a competitor with some of its customers. The balance will be decided by how fast it can run, how good its products are. If they're ahead of everybody else given the demand that's there, everybody will use them still." And you've charted this company for years and you've seen Rene Haas take over as CEO and change the focus, change the emphasis, and try to diversify the revenue streams beyond smartphones where they have that 90 percent market share or above. How much success do you think Rene Haas has had on that front? How do you think this ties in to that pivot? "I mean there's no doubt there's been considerable growth over the last few years. They're now more than 4 billion dollars of annual revenue and that's still growing according to Wall Street estimates. So there's no doubt there's been success there. But at the same time, Nvidia in that period has become a tens if not hundreds of billions of dollar revenue company." "So yes, Arm has done fantastically well and it's been rewarded by the stock market, but compared to what else is available, still a long way to go." And what are you hearing from investors, analysts, and others about Rene Haas as a leader of this business? How comfortable is the board with what Rene Haas has done and the direction he's taken this company in? "I mean, the only person really that he has to keep happy right now is Masa, right? He is the owner of SoftBank who's made this bet on Arm, who bought Arm, put it back into public markets. So you know, the numbers will tell us whether Rene is succeeding or not. Certainly what he's trying to do is in line with what SoftBank is trying to do." "And you know, if they continue to grow and if they continue to snatch part of this opportunity—and let's face it, they don't really have to displace Nvidia—just doubling what they're doing, which is perfectly possible within all of these bits, would be an enormous success." Bloomberg's Ian King. Rene Haas then has spent more than three decades in the tech industry. The Arm CEO started out at Texas Instruments and later spent seven years at Nvidia. He told me the AI boom is unlike anything he's seen before. "It feels different in the sense that it's just much bigger. So if you think about these cycles, whether it was the PCs going to mobile internet, now going to AI, again pattern matching is similar, but the scale is unlike anything we've seen before." "It's really because AI touches every facet of human life, every single facet of human life. When you then combine that with the fact that the amount of compute, memory, and product that's needed to satisfy that demand just has more zeros, the scale is much bigger. That's probably what feels different, and also that the impact is so broad." And you were at Nvidia before generative AI really had its moment. Did you foresee that technology, the embers of that technology, when you were in Nvidia? Were there parts of that story that were coming to play? "Absolutely. And again, back to pattern matching, I was at a semiconductor startup before Nvidia and one of the things we were working on was voice recognition on answering machines." "Now at that point in time, when you think about using voice recognition, it's almost an automatic thing. It works incredibly well. Well, back then, the capability of the computers and the technology to actually recognize voice was very limited, but you sort of see at some point in time it could happen. So fast forward, Nvidia. Did we know AI was going to be the next killer app? No." "But were we investing in areas to allow GPUs for example to be used to program for something other than drawing triangles? Yes. So that's where CUDA was born when I was there in the 2006 time frame. But again, AI is not a new thing. If you go to Bletchley Park and you take a tour and you'll see some of the notes of Alan Turing, he has papers in the 1940s: 'Can machines think?'" "So what's old is new is what's new is old again. Back to the pattern matching, I'm not sure what we're seeing is very new, but what we are seeing is something that I don't think we thought in our lifetime we would see move so quickly." A few years back you told me that one thing that concerned you about AI was potentially humans losing control of the machines. Is that still a concern? "I think the big challenge with AI to your question there is the fact that unlike other areas that can be governed that are physical, whether it's around climate change or weapons, AI is a soft thing. It's software at the end of the day. So how do you manage regulation around software that is fair and equitable, that can be safety checked in such a way that is going to be fair for everyone?" "And that's a hard problem to solve. I think the UK's been doing a good job to try and get in front of this. I know other governments have as well, so it is something I think more needs to be done though." In 10 or 20 years' time when you look back, what do you hope your legacy will have been in this industry? "You know, legacy is not something I think about. In terms of waking up in the morning and looking at everything I need to do and then I look at the Pareto list and say, 'Gosh, should I do that because that might impact my legacy in a negative way?' I just don't think about it that way. People will write what they want to write about what my life was like or what I did professionally and that's just fine. I try to do the best job I can in the moment and the legacy will be whatever it is. It's not something I think about." Arm CEO Rene Haas. And on the next episode of Bloomberg Tech Europe, we're going to take a deep dive into the race for autonomous driving. Catch that episode on May the 8th. The Pulse is up next. This is Bloomberg.

LIVE: IMF Chief Georgieva Delivers Curtain-Raiser Speech Before Spring Meet | N18G

Firstpost(9.5M) ·26:05 ·2.0K views · 2026-04-10
Kristalina Georgieva (Managing Director, IMF)
The global economy is facing a severe supply shock driven by conflict in the Middle East, necessitating a downgrade in growth projections due to energy disruptions, infrastructure damage, and rising inflation.
TL;DR The global economy is facing a severe supply shock driven by conflict in the Middle East, necessitating a downgrade in growth projections due to energy disruptions, infrastructure damage, and rising inflation. Managing Director Kristalina Georgieva urges a policy mix of...

TL;DR

The global economy is facing a severe supply shock driven by conflict in the Middle East, necessitating a downgrade in growth projections due to energy disruptions, infrastructure damage, and rising inflation. Managing Director Kristalina Georgieva urges a policy mix of targeted fiscal support and vigilant monetary policy to prevent an inflationary spiral while rebuilding depleted fiscal buffers.

The Nature and Impact of the Supply Shock

  • Energy Disruptions: The conflict has triggered a massive global supply shock, cutting the world’s daily oil flow by approximately 13% and LNG flow by 20%.
      • Price Volatility: Brent crude rose from $72 to a peak of $120 per barrel; while prices have cooled, they remain at a high premium.
      • Infrastructure Damage: The Ras Laffan complex in Qatar (producing 93% of Gulf LNG) has been offline since early March; it could take three to five years to restore full capacity.
  • Supply Chain Contractions: Red Sea shipping through the Bab el-Mandeb remains at 50% of 2023 levels, causing ripple effects in refined products like diesel and jet fuel.
  • Humanitarian Costs: Higher fertilizer prices and transport restrictions have pushed an additional 45 million people into hunger, bringing the global total to over 360 million.
  • Asymmetric Impact: The shock hits hardest based on geographical proximity to conflict and whether a nation is a net energy importer or exporter.

Economic Outlook and Scenarios

  • Growth Downgrades: Despite prior momentum driven by AI and tech investment, the IMF is lowering growth projections in its forthcoming World Economic Outlook. Georgieva notes that damage to infrastructure and "scarring effects" are already "baked in."
  • Three Transmission Channels:
      • Price Impact: Higher input costs are lifting inflation and reducing consumer demand.
      • Inflation Expectations: While long-term expectations remain stable, short-term curves in the US and Euro area have shifted right, indicating higher uncertainty.
      • Financial Conditions: Emerging market bond spreads have widened, and the US dollar has appreciated, tightening global conditions.
  • Long-term Resilience: The world is progressively less energy-intensive than in the 1980s, which provides a "cushion" for current shocks, though oil remains the primary global fuel.

Regional Vulnerabilities and "Policy Space"

  • The Vulnerability Quadrant: Over 80% of countries are oil importers. The most vulnerable nations—those with low sovereign credit ratings and high import dependency—are concentrated in Sub-Saharan Africa and Pacific Island nations.
  • Policy Buffers: How a country weathers the shock depends on its "policy space" (available reserves and fiscal health). Georgieva warned that many nations have neglected fiscal consolidation during better times, leaving them with limited resources now.
  • Shipping and Transit: Future growth remains hampered by the uncertain recovery of transit through the Strait of Hormuz and regional air traffic.

Policy Recommendations for Leaders

  • Fiscal Discipline: Georgieva cautioned against "pouring gasoline on the fire" by implementing broad-based tax cuts or untargeted energy subsidies.
      • Targeted Support: Fiscal aid must be temporary and directed only at the most vulnerable populations.
      • The "Two Feet" Analogy: Adding deficit-financed stimulus during monetary tightening is "like driving with one foot on the accelerator and the other one on the brake."
  • Monetary Policy: Central banks should remain on hold but must be ready to act decisively with rate hikes if inflation expectations break anchor.
  • Energy Strategy: Countries must maintain a quest for energy efficiency and diversification.
  • Financial Oversight: Regulators must remain nimble. Georgieva specifically warned that if energy insecurity holds back the growth of Artificial Intelligence (due to high power needs), it could trigger a broader financial reversal.
"The reality is we don't truly know what the future holds for transits through the Strait of Hormuz... What we do know is that growth will be slower even if the new peace is durable." — Kristalina Georgieva
"Adding deficit-financed stimulus to this mix at this moment would increase the burden on monetary policy... It would be like driving with one foot on the accelerator and the other one on the brake. Not good." — Kristalina Georgieva
"Take heed: the strength and agility of your fundamentals is your best defense when shocks come. And come they will." — Kristalina Georgieva
It's my job and my great honor this morning to introduce the Managing Director, Dr. Kristalina Georgieva, to help us understand what will be happening next week and give us an economic outlook, particularly with reference to what's going on in Iran and the global economy. We'll have 20 minutes of her thoughts and then we'll open it up to questions, first from me and then from a couple members of our audience. She truly needs no introduction, particularly in this hall, but she's been the Managing Director since 2019, and she was re-elected in 2024. Before that, as you know, she was CEO of the World Bank from 2017 to 2019 where she secured a 13 billion dollar capital increase for the bank at a very difficult time. She is one of the most consequential economic leaders in recent history and it's a great honor to call her a friend as well. Please welcome the Managing Director. A very good morning to all. Good morning! A resilient world economy is being tested again by now, the war in the Middle East. The conflict has caused considerable hardship in the region and around the globe. My heart goes out to all people affected by this war and all wars. Next week we would welcome ministers and central bank governors to our Spring Meetings and our focus will be on how best to weather this latest shock and ease the pain on economies and on people. This requires understanding the nature of the shock, the channels through which it affects the economy, the size of the impact, and the policies that can mitigate it. So, what hit us? A supply shock that is large, with the world's daily oil flow cut by some 13 percent and its LNG flow by some 20 percent. It is global, with all of us now paying more for energy and with supply chains disrupted around the world. And it is asymmetric, with its impact depending on proximity to the conflict, whether you are an energy exporter or importer, and your policy space. As always, a negative supply shock pushes prices up. As a point of reference, Brent jumped from 72 dollars per barrel on the eve of hostilities to a peak of 120 dollars. Thankfully, oil prices have fallen, but they remain much higher than before the war and many countries are paying a high premium for access to precious supplies. Spare a thought for the Pacific Island nations at the end of a long supply chain, wondering if fuel still reaches them in the wake of such severe disruption. The supply interruptions have had and will for some time continue to have ripple effects, such as oil refinery disruptions because of the need to maintain minimum flow rates, with warning lights flashing in many far-flung places. Shortages of refined products including diesel and jet fuel disturb transportation, trade, and tourism in a world that is more interconnected than ever. Food insecurity is pushing another 45 million people into hunger, given the transport restriction problems. This takes the total number of people in hunger to over 360 million, with a problem that is potentially going to get worse over time because of higher fertilizer prices. Supply chain disruptions continue given industrial dependencies on sulfur, on helium for silicon chip making and MRI imaging, and naphtha for plastics. The second question is how can this shock play out? It happens through three main channels. First, the price impact and supply shortages. Higher prices for key inputs feed into many consumer goods, lifting inflation. This, coupled with shortages, reduces demand by a brute force. Second, consider inflation expectations. This can break anchor and ignite a costly inflation process. Been there, seen it. Here is the distribution of near-term inflation forecasts for the US. Notice how the curve has moved to the right, indicating higher short-run inflation expectations. And here is the curve for the Euro area. It also moves to the right and also it widens, indicating higher uncertainty. Now, there is some good news. Fortunately, long-term expectations have not budged. This is very good and very important. Third, look at financial conditions. From a highly supportive starting point, they tightened in an orderly manner. Emerging market bond spreads widened substantially, equity prices have adjusted, and the dollar appreciated. We have been here before in the 70s and earlier this decade. And we know eventually a significant part of the shock will dissipate, leaving us in a new equilibrium. Supply recovers, demand adjusts, new capacity comes on stream, and energy efficiency rises. As a proof, just appreciate how the world has become progressively less energy-intensive since the 80s. And that picture, what you see on the top, this cushions the shock we face today. Renewable energy increased its share, yet oil remains our number one fuel. As the world responds, it is important that we maintain our collective quest for energy efficiency and diversification. Different countries have different paths to energy security, but all must strive for it. Third, how large is the growth impact? The answer very much depends on whether the ceasefire holds and leads to lasting peace, one, and two, how much damage the war leaves in its wake. Given the uncertainties, our World Economic Outlook will be published next week. You can see Pierre-Olivier Gourinchas here on the first row sitting comfortably because the work is done. It would present a range of scenarios going from a relatively swift normalization, which would be of course the best option, to a middle scenario, to one where oil and gas prices stay much higher for much longer and second-round effects take hold. All these scenarios start from a situation where strong AI and tech investment, supportive financial conditions, and other factors were driving considerable momentum in the world economy. In fact, had it not been for this shock, the world would have been in such a much better place and we would have been upgrading our growth projections. But now, even in our most hopeful scenario, we would have a downgrade. Why? Because of significant infrastructure damage, supply disruptions, losses of confidence and other scarring effects. They are already baked in. Take Qatar's Ras Laffan complex, a tremendously important example of strategic investment done right. It is a producer of 93 percent of the Gulf's LNG, with some 80 percent of it going to Asia-Pacific, a region that now endures serious fuel shortages. Ras Laffan has essentially been shut since March 2nd. It took a direct hit on March 19th and could take three to five years to restore to full capacity. Even in the best case, there will be no neat and clean return to the status quo ante. Another relevant fact: note how ship passages through Bab el-Mandeb on the Red Sea have never quite recovered from the devastating disruptions there. They remain stuck at about half of their 2023 level. So the reality is we don't truly know what the future holds for transits through the Strait of Hormuz or, for that matter, for the full recovery of regional air traffic. What we do know is that growth will be slower even if the new peace is durable. And we also know there are significant variations of the impact of this shock around the world, and I really want to dwell on this point. Countries able to export oil and gas undisturbed are the least affected. In contrast, countries directly disturbed by the war, including oil and gas exporters who suffer the blockade and countries relying on imported oil and gas, bear the brunt of the impact. How bad it will be depends in no small measure on how much policy space countries have and how much buffering they can put in action. And that includes also how much they have as oil and gas reserves, given the five-week gap we have seen in tanker traffic from the Gulf. So let me walk you through a few key charts to illustrate three points of differentiation. First, let's separate the world into oil importers on the left—sorry, let me turn this way—on the left and oil exporters on the right. As the pile-up of dots on the left shows, over 80 percent of countries are oil importers. Second, let's highlight countries directly hit by this war. And notice how this disproportionately falls on major oil exporters, although the red dots on the left remind us of the war's toll on regional non-oil economies as well. Third, and I want us to pay attention to this, let's add a vertical scale for countries' sovereign credit rating as a proxy for policy space. The bottom left quadrant is where we find vulnerable oil importers. Let's color Sub-Saharan Africa in yellow and small island nations in orange. Notice how these two sets of countries largely fill that quadrant of vulnerability. And they will be very much the focus of our discussion next week. Now, given that oil is a global commodity, even oil exporters far from the affected region and enjoying terms of trade gains feel the impact of costly oil. So let me say, this is the picture and this is where we are coming from. Let's move to the last and most important question: what should countries do? And I will start with a word of caution up front. This being a classic negative supply shock, demand adjustment is unavoidable. To put it in non-technical language, we cannot go through it without some pain. Policymakers can help to reduce this pain in multiple ways. And certainly, one way in which they can help is: please don't make matters worse. So I appeal to all countries to reject go-it-alone actions, export controls, price controls and so on that can further upset global conditions. Don't pour gasoline on the fire. You need this gasoline to drive your cars. The challenge will be to detect if and when changing conditions take us from one state of the world to another. For now, there is value in waiting and watching, with central banks stressing their commitment to price stability but otherwise staying on hold with a stronger bias to action if credibility is in question. Fiscal authorities should provide targeted and temporary support to the vulnerable, aligned with their medium-term fiscal frameworks. Next, if inflation expectations threaten to break anchor and ignite a costly inflation spiral, then central banks should step in firmly with rate hikes. Fiscal support should remain targeted and temporary. Rate hikes, of course, would further dampen growth. That's how they work and that is the right price to pay for price stability. Finally, in a severe tightening of financial conditions, they add a negative demand shock to the supply shock. Then monetary policy returns to a delicate balancing act while fiscal policy, if and only if there is fiscal space, switches to well-calibrated demand support. So let's see what is actually happening out there. In monetary policy, what are markets telling us? They're telling us that they're expecting major central banks to tighten their policy stance. Here we see four key market-implied policy rate paths, each showing an upward shift. In energy policy we see many countries putting in place emergency conservation measures, from general campaigns to limits on private vehicle use to remote work. These and other steps are well-documented by the International Energy Agency. By the way, this is the reason we formed a partnership: the World Bank, International Energy Agency and the Fund, where we concentrate on the macro side. And what this graph tells us is that in terms of economic support measures, quite a number of countries are doing the right thing. They're going with targeted measures; they're shaping them to be temporary. But then we also have broad-based measures, those that are in red, and while we haven't done a detailed analysis, it does worry me that there is so much red on this slide. So let me move to looking into what is the step we need to take on the fiscal policy front. We have to avoid untargeted tax cuts; we have to avoid energy subsidies that are not well-targeted. And I cannot stress enough, we have to do it recognizing how limited fiscal space we have. We will point out that measures that mute the price signal also mute the necessary demand response, resulting in higher global energy prices. And we will work with countries to make sure that as the situation evolves, they have the agility to correct their policy stance appropriately. So let me also look at another part of reality: what is happening on the financial front. Already the world has seen benchmark yield curves rising, driving up the cost of debt. Adding deficit-financed stimulus to this mix at this moment would increase the burden on monetary policy and amplify such shifts. It would be like driving with one foot on the accelerator and the other one on the brake. Not good. As we will flag in our forthcoming Fiscal Monitor, the world does have a fiscal space problem. Public debt is generally much higher than 20 years ago, including in most G20 countries, reflecting widespread neglect of fiscal consolidation in the periods when conditions permitted it. As a result, interest payments are rising as a share of revenues at all income levels. And the implication is clear: all countries must deploy their fiscal resources responsibly and most must move decisively to rebuilding fiscal space after this shock. And I cannot emphasize this enough. And let me say a word about financial sector policies. As our Global Financial Stability Report—Tobias Adrian is right here—would insist, it is essential that financial regulators and supervisors are alert, nimble and responsive to a fluid situation. Financial conditions have been highly accommodative for some time, spurred by tech optimism and new financial intermediaries, many of them non-banks. While this has lifted growth, it also creates risks of reversal. If investors were to start worrying about energy insecurity, holding back the growth of AI given AI's huge energy needs, then we could find ourselves in a spot of trouble. Micro and macroprudential policies must work to reduce financial stability risks and ensure a resilient system. With that, I want to stress the most important lesson of all: good policies make a difference. There are forces countries cannot control, but they do have authority over their own policies and institutions. Take heed: the strength and agility of your fundamentals is your best defense when shocks come. And come they will. And as you deal with the long tail of the current shock, do not forget to steer the great global transformations in technology, in demographics, in geopolitics...
